[ رمز الحقائق: ٣٤١/٢] (طائي) ولم يشتر إلخ : أي ليس للمضارب أن يشتري من يعتق على المالك أي على رب المال بقرابة كأبيه وابنه أو بسبب يمين؛ لكونه مخالفا للمقصود بخلاف الوكيل بشراء العبد، حيث يجوز له أن يشتري من يعتق على الموكل؛ لأن التوكيل مطلق فيجري على إطلاقه وهنا مقيد بمال يمكن التجارة فيه، حتى لو وجد في الوكالة أيضا ما يدل على التقييد بأن قال : اشتر لي عبداً أبيعه أو أستخدمه، أو جارية أطؤها، كان الحكم كذلك، ولو اشترى من يعتق على رب المال صار مشترياً لنفسه، ويضمنه؛ لأنه نقد الثمن من المضاربة، وعند مالك لو كان عالماً موسراً ضمن وإلا لا . [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٤١/٢] قال في "الفتح": ومقتضاه الضمان عندنا مطلقاً، سواء كان عالماً موسراً أو لا. لأنه مال ربح؛ يعتق نصيبه، ويفسد بسببه أو عليه إلخ: أي ليس للمضارب أن يشتري من يعتق عليه إن ظهر في المال. نصيب رب المال، أو يعتق على الاختلاف الذي مضى بيانه في العتق والمراد من ظهور الربح أن يكون قيمة العبد المشترى أكثر من رأس المال، سواء كان في جملة رأس المال ربح أو لا ؛ لأنه إذا كان قيمة العبد مثل رأس المال أو أقل، لا يظهر ملك المضارب فيه، بل يجعل مشغولا برأس المال حتى إذا كان رأس المال ألفا، وصار عشرة آلاف درهم، ثم اشترى المضارب من يعتق عليه وقيمته ألف أو أقل، لا يعتق عليه، وكذا لو كان له ثلاثة أولاد أو أكثر، وقيمة كل واحد ألف أو أقل، فاشتراه، لا يعتق منهم شيء؛ لأن كل واحد مشغول برأس المال؛ ولا يملك المضارب شيئاً، حتى يزيد قيمة كل عين على رأس المال على حدة من غير ضمه إلى آخر. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٢/٢] منهم
'And he did not exceed, etc.': [Beginning of specialized mudarabah] i.e., the mudarib did not transgress what the capital owner specified: a specific country, such as saying 'do not work except in Egypt', a specific commodity such as 'do not buy or sell except silk', a specific time such as 'do not buy or sell except at such time', and a specific person such as 'do not work except with Zaid bin so-and-so'. Then if he exceeds, he is liable, and if he did not buy anything until he returns the property to the country specified for him, he is freed from liability like a depositor who violated in the deposit, then returned to compliance, and the property returns to mudarabah so that if he buys in that country, it is for the mudarabah. This differs from if he restricted him to a specific market in the city, where he is not restricted by it, because the single city hardly differs in its districts and markets, as it is like one place, so the restriction is not beneficial unless he explicitly forbids by saying 'work in this market and do not work in others', then he is restricted by it, because the property is his, and the authority of disposal belongs to him, unlike if he said to him 'sell on credit and do not sell for cash' when there is no difference in price between them, because it is a violation to a better with certainty, like one who authorized a person to sell his slave for a thousand dirhams and forbade him from selling for more, and the agent sold for two thousand, then it is permissible due to what we said. Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/241] At [PAGE_095] about what he specified of country, commodity, time, or person as in the partnership, and he did not buy from the capital owner by saying 'take this property' that becomes free upon the owner, or upon him if profit appears, by kinship or oath of the mudarib immediately, a specific person, i.e., in a restricted partnership, i.e., the mudarib originally, like his prohibition from selling the property. As for the beneficial in the property, meaning from a country because mudarabah accepts beneficial restriction, even after the contract as long as the property has not become goods, because then he cannot remove him, so he cannot specify him, as will come. And we restricted it to beneficial because non-beneficial is not considered at all, like a market in a city, for if he explicitly forbids, it is valid, otherwise not, as in al-Durar. And his statement: 'like his prohibition from selling when there is no difference in price, as in Sharh al-‘Ayni. (Fath Allah al-Mu‘in) Or person, what is meant is a specific person; because if he said 'on condition that you buy from the people of Kufa' or said 'on condition that you work in currency exchange and buy from the money-changers and sell to them', and he sold in Kufa to a man not from the people of Kufa or from other than money-changers, it is permissible; because the purpose of this speech is restriction by place or type, so that he is permitted to leave Kufa in the first and sell there to its people or others, and he is not permitted to work in other than exchange in the second, and he may buy and sell from money-changers and others, because restriction by place and type is beneficial, and restriction to the people of Kufa and money-changers is not beneficial, because each of them is a large group that cannot be enumerated, as in al-Zayla‘i. (Fath, Mulla Miskin) 'As in the partnership', i.e., just as it is not permissible for the partner in a restricted partnership to transgress what the other partner specified in any of it. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/341] (Ta'i) 'And he did not buy, etc.': i.e., it is not for the mudarib to buy the one who becomes free upon the owner, i.e., upon the capital owner, due to kinship like his father or son, or due to an oath; because it contradicts the intended purpose, unlike an agent for buying a slave, where he is permitted to buy the one who becomes free upon the principal; because the agency is absolute, so it operates absolutely, and here it is restricted to property that can be traded, so even if in the agency there is found what indicates restriction, such as saying 'buy for me a slave to sell or use, or a female slave to have intercourse with', the ruling is the same. And if he bought the one who becomes free upon the capital owner, he becomes a buyer for himself, and he is liable for it, because he paid the price from the mudarabah. And according to Malik, if he was knowledgeable and solvent, he is liable, otherwise not. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/241] He said in al-Fath: Its implication is liability with us absolutely, whether he is knowledgeable and solvent or not. Because it is property of profit; he emancipates his share, and it is corrupted due to it. 'Or upon him, etc.': i.e., it is not for the mudarib to buy the one who becomes free upon him if profit appears in the property. The portion of the capital owner, or becomes free according to the difference that has passed in emancipation, and the meaning of appearance of profit is that the value of the purchased slave is more than the capital, whether there is profit in the total capital or not; because if the value of the slave equals the capital or less, the ownership of the mudarib does not appear in it, rather it remains occupied by the capital, so that if the capital was one thousand, and it became ten thousand dirhams, then the mudarib bought the one who becomes free upon him whose value is one thousand or less, it does not become free upon him. Likewise, if he had three or more children, and the value of each is one thousand or less, and he bought them, none of them becomes free; because each one is occupied by the capital, and the mudarib does not own anything until the value of each item exceeds the capital separately without combining with another. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/242] from them.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage details the restrictions a capital owner may impose on the mudarib, and the consequences of exceeding them. The owner may restrict the mudarib to a specific country, commodity, time, or specific transaction partner. If the mudarib acts contrary to these, he becomes liable for any loss. However, if he refrains from buying and returns the capital to the specified country, he reverts to the original mudarabah contract. A key point: restriction to a specific market within one city is not effective unless the owner explicitly forbids other markets, because a city is considered a single place. Similarly, if the owner says 'sell on credit' when there is no price difference, the mudarib may still sell for cash if it is better, analogized to an agent selling for more than the stated price. The second part deals with buying a person who would become free upon the owner (e.g., a close relative). The mudarib cannot do this because it defeats the purpose of profit-seeking; he would be using capital to emancipate, not trade. If he does, he buys for himself and is liable. This is unlike an absolute agent. Regarding profit: if the mudarib buys a relative using capital, the emancipation only occurs if the slave's value exceeds the capital; if not, the slave remains occupied by capital and does not become free. Each relative is considered separately.

Key Terms
المضاربة الخاصة (al-mudarabah al-khassah): A mudarabah with specific restrictions imposed by the capital owner.
التقييد المفيد (al-taqyid al-mufid): A restriction that has a beneficial effect, such as to a country or type of goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a mudarib is restricted to trade only in Egypt but first goes to Syria without buying, returns to Egypt, and then buys?
A) He is liable for any loss because he entered Syria.
B) He is not liable because he returned before buying.
C) He is liable only if the Syrian trip caused a loss.
D) The mudarabah is void automatically.

Key Takeaway
A mudarib only becomes liable for violating beneficial restrictions; non-beneficial restrictions require explicit prohibition to be binding.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
advanced
10 min
Mudarib Buying Relatives of the Capital Owner
Ruling on buying emancipatable relatives and condition of profit for emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition on a mudarib buying a relative of the capital owner, determine when liability arises, and explain the profit condition for emancipation.

Core Idea
A mudarib cannot buy a person who would become free upon the capital owner, as it defeats the purpose of mudarabah; if he does, he buys for himself and is liable.

The mudarib is entrusted with capital for trade, not for emancipation. If he purchases a slave related to the capital owner (e.g., father, son), the purchase is for himself, not the mudarabah. This lesson details the ruling, the condition of profit, and compares with absolute agency and the Maliki position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited purchase
It is not permitted for the mudarib to buy a person who would become free upon the capital owner due to kinship (e.g., father, son) or due to a previous oath (e.g., if the owner swore to free a particular slave).
This includes any slave related to the owner within the prohibited degrees of marriage.
2
Understand the rationale
The purpose of mudarabah is to generate profit through trade. Buying a relative defeats this purpose because the slave would become free immediately or upon profit, thus the capital is used for emancipation, not trade.
In contrast, an absolute agent (wakeel mutlaq) is allowed to buy such relatives because the agency is unrestricted.
3
Determine the consequence of purchase
If the mudarib buys such a relative, the purchase is considered for himself, not for the mudarabah. He must pay the price from his own pocket and is liable for the capital spent.
The text says: 'he becomes a buyer for himself, and he is liable for it, because he paid the price from the mudarabah.'
4
Apply the profit condition for emancipation
Even if the mudarib buys the relative using mudarabah capital, emancipation only occurs if the value of the slave exceeds the capital. If the value is equal to or less than the capital, the slave does not become free.
For example, capital 1000, slave value 800: no emancipation. If capital 1000, slave value 1200: emancipation occurs only for the owner's share? Actually, the text says each relative is considered separately.
5
Note the Maliki difference
According to Malik, the mudarib is only liable if he was knowledgeable (of the relationship) and solvent (able to pay). Otherwise, the purchase is valid for the mudarabah. Abu Hanifa holds absolute liability regardless.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is absolute liability.

'And he did not exceed, etc.': [Beginning of specialized mudarabah] i.e., the mudarib did not transgress what the capital owner specified: a specific country, such as saying 'do not work except in Egypt', a specific commodity such as 'do not buy or sell except silk', a specific time such as 'do not buy or sell except at such time', and a specific person such as 'do not work except with Zaid bin so-and-so'. Then if he exceeds, he is liable, and if he did not buy anything until he returns the property to the country specified for him, he is freed from liability like a depositor who violated in the deposit, then returned to compliance, and the property returns to mudarabah so that if he buys in that country, it is for the mudarabah. This differs from if he restricted him to a specific market in the city, where he is not restricted by it, because the single city hardly differs in its districts and markets, as it is like one place, so the restriction is not beneficial unless he explicitly forbids by saying 'work in this market and do not work in others', then he is restricted by it, because the property is his, and the authority of disposal belongs to him, unlike if he said to him 'sell on credit and do not sell for cash' when there is no difference in price between them, because it is a violation to a better with certainty, like one who authorized a person to sell his slave for a thousand dirhams and forbade him from selling for more, and the agent sold for two thousand, then it is permissible due to what we said. As for the beneficial in the property, meaning from a country because mudarabah accepts beneficial restriction, even after the contract as long as the property has not become goods, because then he cannot remove him, so he cannot specify him, as will come. And we restricted it to beneficial because non-beneficial is not considered at all, like a market in a city, for if he explicitly forbids, it is valid, otherwise not, as in al-Durar. And his statement: 'like his prohibition from selling when there is no difference in price, as in Sharh al-‘Ayni. (Fath Allah al-Mu‘in) Or person, what is meant is a specific person; because if he said 'on condition that you buy from the people of Kufa' or said 'on condition that you work in currency exchange and buy from the money-changers and sell to them', and he sold in Kufa to a man not from the people of Kufa or from other than money-changers, it is permissible; because the purpose of this speech is restriction by place or type, so that he is permitted to leave Kufa in the first and sell there to its people or others, and he is not permitted to work in other than exchange in the second, and he may buy and sell from money-changers and others, because restriction by place and type is beneficial, and restriction to the people of Kufa and money-changers is not beneficial, because each of them is a large group that cannot be enumerated, as in al-Zayla‘i. (Fath, Mulla Miskin) 'As in the partnership', i.e., just as it is not permissible for the partner in a restricted partnership to transgress what the other partner specified in any of it. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/341] (Ta'i) 'And he did not buy, etc.': i.e., it is not for the mudarib to buy the one who becomes free upon the owner, i.e., upon the capital owner, due to kinship like his father or son, or due to an oath; because it contradicts the intended purpose, unlike an agent for buying a slave, where he is permitted to buy the one who becomes free upon the principal; because the agency is absolute, so it operates absolutely, and here it is restricted to property that can be traded, so even if in the agency there is found what indicates restriction, such as saying 'buy for me a slave to sell or use, or a female slave to have intercourse with', the ruling is the same. Because it is property of profit; he emancipates his share, and it is corrupted due to it. 'Or upon him, etc.': i.e., it is not for the mudarib to buy the one who becomes free upon him if profit appears in the property. The portion of the capital owner, or becomes free according to the difference that has passed in emancipation, and the meaning of appearance of profit is that the value of the purchased slave is more than the capital, whether there is profit in the total capital or not; because if the value of the slave equals the capital or less, the ownership of the mudarib does not appear in it, rather it remains occupied by the capital, so that if the capital was one thousand, and it became ten thousand dirhams, then the mudarib bought the one who becomes free upon him whose value is one thousand or less, it does not become free upon him. Likewise, if he had three or more children, and the value of each is one thousand or less, and he bought them, none of them becomes free; because each one is occupied by the capital, and the mudarib does not own anything until the value of each item exceeds the capital separately without combining with another. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/242] from them.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage details the restrictions a capital owner may impose on the mudarib, and the consequences of exceeding them. The owner may restrict the mudarib to a specific country, commodity, time, or specific transaction partner. If the mudarib acts contrary to these, he becomes liable for any loss. However, if he refrains from buying and returns the capital to the specified country, he reverts to the original mudarabah contract. A key point: restriction to a specific market within one city is not effective unless the owner explicitly forbids other markets, because a city is considered a single place. Similarly, if the owner says 'sell on credit' when there is no price difference, the mudarib may still sell for cash if it is better, analogized to an agent selling for more than the stated price. The second part deals with buying a person who would become free upon the owner (e.g., a close relative). The mudarib cannot do this because it defeats the purpose of profit-seeking; he would be using capital to emancipate, not trade. If he does, he buys for himself and is liable. This is unlike an absolute agent. Regarding profit: if the mudarib buys a relative using capital, the emancipation only occurs if the slave's value exceeds the capital; if not, the slave remains occupied by capital and does not become free. Each relative is considered separately.

Key Terms
يعتق على المالك (ya‘tiq ‘ala al-malik): A slave who becomes free due to kinship (e.g., father, son) with the capital owner.
المضاربة الخاصة (al-mudarabah al-khassah): A mudarabah with specific restrictions imposed by the capital owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a mudarib become liable for buying a relative of the capital owner?
A) Only if he knew about the relationship.
B) Only if he had the means to pay.
C) Absolutely, regardless of knowledge or solvency.
D) Only if the transaction resulted in a loss.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib is absolutely prohibited from buying a relative of the capital owner; if he does, he must personally compensate the capital, and emancipation only occurs if the slave's value exceeds the capital.

Mudarib Cannot Buy Slave Who Would Free on Owner
Ruling and proof for prohibition on mudarib buying a relative of the owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a mudarib buying a relative of the capital owner and explain the distinction from a general agent in terms of liability.

Core Idea
A mudarib may not buy a slave who would be freed on the owner; such a purchase is void and the mudarib is personally liable.

In the previous lesson, we learned the basic rules of mudarabah. Here, the text expands on a specific prohibition: the mudarib cannot purchase a slave who would automatically be freed upon the capital owner due to kinship. Why? Because mudarabah is restricted to trade, and such a purchase defeats the purpose. Let's unpack the ruling and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited act
Read the passage and identify what exactly the mudarib is prohibited from buying: a slave who would be freed upon the capital owner (rabb al-mal) due to kinship (like father or son) or an oath.
2
Understand the reason for prohibition
The text states it is 'contrary to the purpose.' The purpose of mudarabah is trade for profit. Buying a relative who will be freed does not generate trade profit and wastes capital.
3
Contrast with a general agent (wakil)
A general agent may buy such a slave because his agency is unrestricted. The mudarib's authority is implicitly restricted to trade, hence the difference.
4
Determine the consequence of violation
If the mudarib violates, he becomes the purchaser for himself and must compensate the capital owner for the price paid from the mudarabah capital.
5
Note the Maliki disagreement and Hanafi position
Imam Malik conditions liability on the mudarib being aware and wealthy. The Hanafi school, as per al-Fath, holds liability absolute regardless.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يشتر إلخ : أي ليس للمضارب أن يشتري من يعتق على المالك أي على رب المال بقرابة كأبيه وابنه أو بسبب يمين؛ لكونه مخالفا للمقصود بخلاف الوكيل بشراء العبد، حيث يجوز له أن يشتري من يعتق على الموكل؛ لأن التوكيل مطلق فيجري على إطلاقه وهنا مقيد بمال يمكن التجارة فيه، حتى لو وجد في الوكالة أيضا ما يدل على التقييد بأن قال : اشتر لي عبداً أبيعه أو أستخدمه، أو جارية أطؤها، كان الحكم كذلك، ولو اشترى من يعتق على رب المال صار مشترياً لنفسه، ويضمنه؛ لأنه نقد الثمن من المضاربة، وعند مالك لو كان عالماً موسراً ضمن وإلا لا . [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٤١/٢] قال في "الفتح": ومقتضاه الضمان عندنا مطلقاً، سواء كان عالماً موسراً أو لا.
And he does not buy etc.: i.e., it is not for the mudarib that he buys one who would be freed upon the owner, i.e., upon the rabb al-mal, due to kinship like his father and his son, or due to an oath; because it is contrary to the purpose, unlike the agent (wakil) in purchasing a slave, where it is permissible for him to buy one who would be freed upon the principal; because the agency is absolute and is executed according to its absoluteness, and here it is restricted to wealth with which trade can be conducted. Even if in the agency there is also an indication of restriction, such as if he said: 'Buy me a slave to sell or employ,' or 'a female slave for intercourse,' the ruling would be likewise. And if he buys one who would be freed upon the rabb al-mal, he becomes a purchaser for himself, and he guarantees it; because he paid the price from the mudarabah. According to Malik, if he was aware and wealthy, he guarantees; otherwise no. [Ramus al-Haqaiq: 241/2] He said in al-Fath: 'Its implication is the guarantee in our school absolutely, whether he is aware and wealthy or not.'
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's phrase 'ولم يشتر' (and he does not buy). It states a ruling from the chapter of mudarabah. The mudarib (agent) is prohibited from purchasing a slave who would be set free upon the rabb al-mal (capital owner) due to kinship (e.g., father or son) or a right of wala' arising from an oath. The reason given is that such a purchase contradicts the purpose of mudarabah, which is trade. In contrast, a general agent (wakil) is allowed to buy such a person because his agency is unrestricted. However, if the agent's appointment is also implicitly restricted (e.g., buying a slave for resale or use), the same prohibition applies. If the mudarib violates this, the purchase is for himself, not the partnership, and he is liable for the price paid from the mudarabah capital. The commentary then notes a difference with Imam Malik: Malik requires the mudarib to be both aware and wealthy to incur liability; otherwise not. The author of al-Fath states that in the Hanafi school, liability is absolute regardless of knowledge or wealth. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Key Terms
مضارب (mudārib): The agent or partner in a mudarabah contract who manages the capital and shares in the profit.
رب المال (rabb al-māl): The capital owner in a mudarabah contract who provides the money and receives a share of profit.
يعتق (yuʿtiq): To be set free; here, the slave would be automatically freed upon the owner due to kinship or wala'.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; the obligation to compensate for the price paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mudarib buys a slave who is the father of the rabb al-mal, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The purchase is valid, and the slave is free.
B) The mudarib becomes the purchaser for himself and must pay the price.
C) The purchase is valid, and the mudarib is not liable if he was unaware.
D) The slave is free, and the mudarib is not liable if he is poor.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's authority is limited to trade; any purchase that defeats this purpose is void and the mudarib is personally liable.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
8 min
Mudarib Cannot Purchase a Slave Who Becomes Free Upon Profit
Ruling on mudarib not buying a slave who would be free upon him

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition on a mudarib buying a slave who would be freed upon himself if profit appears, and apply the condition that profit is measured per item value exceeding capital.

Core Idea
A mudarib is prohibited from buying any slave who would be freed upon himself if the slave's value exceeds the capital.

This lesson expands on the matn's phrase 'or upon himself'. It clarifies that the mudarib is also barred from purchasing a slave who, due to his lineage, would be set free upon the mudarib's ownership if profit appears. The key is understanding when profit 'appears'—it requires the slave's value to individually exceed the capital. This prevents conflict of interest in the mudarabah partnership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
The mudarib cannot buy a slave who would be free upon himself if profit appears. This is an extension of the general rule that the mudarib must not conflict with the partnership interests.
2
Define 'appearance of profit'
Profit appears when the value of the purchased slave exceeds the original capital, regardless of overall profit in the mudarabah. If value equals or is less, no profit appears for that item.
The mudarib's ownership share only emerges on the excess value.
3
Apply the condition per item
Each slave's value must be assessed individually. If a mudarib buys multiple slaves each worth less than or equal to capital, none become free because each is 'occupied by the capital'.
Example: capital 2000, three slaves each worth 1000 → none free.
4
Determine consequences
If the mudarib violates and buys such a slave, he is liable. If no profit appears at purchase, the purchase is valid. If profit later appears, his share of the slave becomes free automatically, and he does not compensate the rabb al-mal for that share.
The freedom is by operation of law, not deliberate act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو عليه إلخ: أي ليس للمضارب أن يشتري من يعتق عليه إن ظهر في المال. نصيب رب المال، أو يعتق على الاختلاف الذي مضى بيانه في العتق والمراد من ظهور الربح أن يكون قيمة العبد المشترى أكثر من رأس المال، سواء كان في جملة رأس المال ربح أو لا ؛ لأنه إذا كان قيمة العبد مثل رأس المال أو أقل، لا يظهر ملك المضارب فيه، بل يجعل مشغولا برأس المال حتى إذا كان رأس المال ألفا، وصار عشرة آلاف درهم، ثم اشترى المضارب من يعتق عليه وقيمته ألف أو أقل، لا يعتق عليه، وكذا لو كان له ثلاثة أولاد أو أكثر، وقيمة كل واحد ألف أو أقل، فاشتراه، لا يعتق منهم شيء؛ لأن كل واحد مشغول برأس المال؛ ولا يملك المضارب شيئاً، حتى يزيد قيمة كل عين على رأس المال على حدة من غير ضمه إلى آخر. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٢/٢] منهم وضمن إن فعل، فإن لم يظهر ربح صح، فإن ظهر عتق حظه، ولم يضمن لرب المال،

The meaning of the appearance of profit is that the value of the purchased slave be more than the capital, whether there is profit in the total capital or not; because if the value of the slave is equal to the capital or less, the ownership of the mudarib does not appear in it, but it is considered occupied by the capital. So if the capital is two thousand and becomes ten thousand dirhams, then the mudarib buys one who would be freed upon himself and its value is one thousand or less, it does not free upon him. [Ramus al-Haqaiq: 242/2] From them. And he is liable if he does it. If no profit appears, the purchase is valid. If profit appears, his share becomes free, and he does not guarantee to the rabb al-mal.

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn's phrase 'أو عليه' (or upon himself). It states that the mudarib is also prohibited from buying a slave who would be set free upon himself (the mudarib) if profit appears in the mudarabah capital. The key condition is the 'appearance of profit', meaning the slave's value must exceed the original capital. If the value is equal to or less than the capital, the mudarib does not actually own any share of the slave because the entire value is considered as covering the capital. For example, if the capital was 2000 and later became 10,000, and the mudarib buys a slave worth 1000 or less, that slave does not become free; it is still burdened by the capital. Similarly, if he buys multiple children each worth 1000 or less, none become free. Profit must be assessed per item, i.e., each slave's value must individually exceed the capital. The commentary then states: if the mudarib does buy such a slave, he is liable (he guarantees). However, if no profit appears (i.e., the slave's value does not exceed capital), the purchase is valid for the mudarabah. If profit does appear later, the mudarib's share (e.g., half) becomes free, and he does not compensate the rabb al-mal for that portion. This is because the freedom occurs by operation of law due to the value increase, not by his deliberate action.

Key Terms
ظهور الربح (ẓuhūr al-ribḥ): The appearance of profit; here, the condition that the value of a specific item exceeds the original capital, enabling the mudarib to own a share.
مشغول برأس المال (mashghūl bi-raʾs al-māl): Occupied by the capital; meaning the item's value is considered as covering the capital, so the mudarib has no ownership yet.
حظه (ḥiẓẓuhu): His share (the mudarib's share of the profit, which becomes free upon him).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mudarabah with capital 5,000, the mudarib purchases a slave worth 6,000 who is his father. The purchase occurs. What is the ruling?
A) The purchase is valid and the slave is free because profit appeared.
B) The purchase is prohibited, and the mudarib is liable. If profit appears, the mudarib's share becomes free and he does not compensate.
C) The purchase is valid only if the mudarib guarantees the rabb al-mal's share.
D) The purchase is invalid, and the mudarib must return the slave to the seller.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib must not acquire any asset that would grant him personal freedom rights if its value exceeds the capital, as it compromises the partnership.

Mudarib Fathers Child with Umm Walad: Two Options for Capital Owner
Case ruling on mudarib's paternity claim and child's freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the child of an umm walad in a mudarabah contract, specifically stating the two options for the rabb al-mal.

Core Idea
When a mudarib fathers a child with an umm walad bought with partnership capital, the child may be freed only after compensating the capital owner.

This case from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents a delicate intersection of mudarabah and slavery law. The mudarib, using partnership funds, buys an umm walad and later claims paternity. The child's legal status is complicated because it is partly owned by the rabb al-mal. The Hanafi ruling provides two clear options for the capital owner, balancing the mudarib's paternal claim and the investor's property rights.

Guided Path
1
Establish the scenario
Identify all parties: mudarib, rabb al-mal, capital (1000 dirhams), umm walad, child, paternity claim. Note the mudarib's wealth condition.
The mudarib is 'mussir' (wealthy) at the time of claim.
2
Determine the child's ownership and value
The child is owned proportionally by the mudarib and rabb al-mal because the umm walad was purchased with partnership capital. The child's value increases from 1000 to 1500.
The mudarib's claim frees his own share in the child.
3
Apply the rabb al-mal's options
The rabb al-mal may either (1) require the child to work (sa'ā) to pay the rabb al-mal's share of the value (1000 + 250 = 1250 dirhams), or (2) free the child outright if he wishes.
The child's labour compensates for the rabb al-mal's lost property.
4
Reason under the principle
The mudarib's paternity claim (istilḥāq) frees the child, but since the child is partially owned by the rabb al-mal, compensation is required. The rule protects the investor's rights.
This harmonizes istilḥāq with the mudarabah partnership.

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed case study from the rulings of mudarabah regarding an umm walad (a female slave who bears a child from her owner). The scenario: The mudarib receives 1000 dirhams as mudarabah capital on a half-profit share. He uses that capital to buy an umm walad worth 1000 dirhams. He then has intercourse with her, and she gives birth to a child worth 1000 dirhams. The mudarib claims the child as his own while he is wealthy (i.e., can afford to buy the child's freedom). The child's value later increases to 1500 dirhams. The ruling: The rabb al-mal (capital owner) has two options: (1) He may require the child to work (sā'ā) to pay off the owner's share of the value, which is 1000 plus one quarter (250), totalling 1250 dirhams. (2) He may choose to free the child (set it free). The commentary explains that 'mudarabah by half' means the mudarib gets half the profit. Here, the mudarib's claim of paternity (istilḥāq) makes the child free if the mudarib is wealthy, but since the child is partly owned by the rabb al-mal (due to the mudarabah), the rabb al-mal can either seek compensation through the child's labor or free him outright.

Key Terms
أمة (ama): A female slave; here specifically an umm walad because the mudarib claims paternity.
ادعاه (iddaʿāhu): He claimed it (the child) as his own; the mudarib asserts paternity (istilḥāq).
سعى (saʿā): He worked; the child is required to work (mukātab) to pay off the rabb al-mal's share.

Key Takeaway
The child of an umm walad purchased with mudarabah capital is partially owned by the rabb al-mal; the mudarib's paternity claim requires compensation to free the child completely.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

The Mudarib's Paternity Claim and the Principle of Profit Realization
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mudarib's paternity claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions under which a mudarib's paternity claim is valid in Hanafi law and distinguish between ikhbar (report) and insha' (contract) in this context.

Core Idea
A mudarib's paternity claim only takes effect after he actually owns a share of the child through realized profit.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a mudarib who claims paternity of a child born from a slave-girl purchased with mudarabah capital. The key question is: when does such a claim become valid? The Hanafi masters agree that the claim is not valid immediately; it depends on the realization of profit and the mudarib's ownership of a share. This leads to important distinctions between types of legal acts (ikhbar vs. insha') and types of guarantee (daman al-ifsad vs. daman al-tamalluk), which you will explore step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Scenario
First, parse the scenario: mudarib takes 1000 dirhams capital for half-profit, buys a slave-girl worth 1000, has intercourse, she bears a child worth 1000, later child's value rises to 1500. Mudarib claims paternity while wealthy. Identify the legal elements: mudarabah, paternity claim, potential profit.
The child's value increase is crucial; profit is realized when value exceeds capital encumbrance.
2
Identify the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Note the differing views: Abu Hanifa holds the purchase is fasid (corrupt) due to conflict of interest; Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is sahih (valid). The text proceeds on Sahibayn's view, assuming validity. Also note Zufar's contrary opinion on profit realization.
The dispute affects when the mudarib owns a share.
3
Trace the Condition of Profit Realization
The Hanafi principle: when mudarabah goods are of different types (e.g., slave-girl and child), profit is not realized until all goods are sold or until the value of each exceeds cost. Here, initially no profit appears because capital encumbers both the mother and child. Only when the child's value reaches 1500 does profit emerge in that asset, giving the mudarib a share (half of the increase from 1000 to 1500 = 250). Then the paternity claim becomes valid because ownership exists.
Contrast with Zufar: profit appears in each type individually.
4
Distinguish Ikhbar vs. Insha'
The paternity claim (da'wa) is an ikhbar (report) about a past fact (lineage). It is valid retrospectively: if the mudarib later acquires ownership, the earlier claim takes effect. In contrast, emancipation (itaq) is an insha' (contract) that requires present ownership; if done before ownership, it is void and cannot be revived later. This explains why the claim can become effective after profit realization.
Key legal maxim: 'Duties attach to the later description.' (al-hukm yudaf ila al-wasf al-akhir)
5
Apply the Different Types of Guarantee
The text distinguishes between daman al-ifsad (guarantee for damage, e.g., unauthorized emancipation) and daman al-tamalluk (guarantee for ownership, e.g., taking over property). The mudarib is liable for daman al-tamalluk (half the slave-girl's value) when the owner takes the child's compensation, but not for daman al-ifsad (emancipation) because he did not act wrongfully. Wealth (yasar) affects daman al-ifsad but not daman al-tamalluk.
The owner has a choice: either demand the child to work for compensation or emancipate him.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of a mudarib's paternity claim on a child born from mudarabah property. The scenario: The mudarib takes 1000 dirhams as mudarabah capital with a half-profit share. He buys a slave-girl worth 1000, has intercourse with her, and she gives birth to a child worth 1000. The mudarib claims the child as his son while he is wealthy. The child's value then increases to 1500. The text discusses the options for the owner of capital (rabb al-mal): he may demand the child to work for compensation (sua) or emancipate him. If the owner demands the 1000 (plus quarter?), the mudarib is liable for half the slave-girl's value. The commentary explains that the paternity claim is valid only after profit is realized, because initially the mudarib does not own the child due to the capital encumbrance. This follows the Hanafi principle that in mudarabah, if goods of different types increase in value, profit is not realized until all are sold, contrary to Zufar. The text also distinguishes between a claim (ikhabar) and a contract (insha), such as emancipation. The mudarib's claim is retrospective, so when he later owns a share, it becomes effective. The owner is given a choice: to emancipate his share or demand compensation. The commentary also covers cases where the owner of capital claims paternity, which takes precedence. Important legal subtleties include the distinction between guarantee for damages (daman al-ifsad) and guarantee for ownership (daman al-tamalluk), and that the mudarib is not liable for emancipation since it occurred without his deliberate act.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership in which one party provides capital and the other provides labor/expertise, sharing profits according to agreement.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The owner of the capital in a mudarabah contract, who provides funds to the mudarib.
مضارب (mudarib): The partner who manages the business using the capital provided by the owner.
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim, here specifically the mudarib's assertion that a child is his son.
إخبار (ikhbar): A report or statement of fact, which can be retroactively validated when later proven true.
إنشاء (insha'): A contract or declaration that creates a new legal effect, requiring present capacity and ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A mudarib's paternity claim is conditionally valid only after he acquires ownership of a share through realized profit, with important distinctions between reports (ikhbar) and contracts (insha').

Mudarib's Claim of Paternity and Capital Recovery
Detailed commentary on mudarib's claim of paternity and its legal effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a mudarib's claim of paternity is only effective after capital recovery and evaluate Zufar's opposing view.

Core Idea
A mudarib’s claim of paternity only takes effect when capital is recovered, because profit does not appear until the capital is freed.

In mudarabah, the mudarib may claim paternity of a child born from a slave woman purchased with partnership capital. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling: the claim is initially ineffective because the assets are encumbered by capital. Only when the value of the child exceeds the capital and profit appears does the claim become executable. The passage also highlights a dispute within the Hanafi school, contrasting the majority view with that of Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the mudarib's claim of paternity over a child born from a slave woman purchased with mudarabah capital. The ruling is that the claim is not immediately effective.
The claim is treated as a report (ikhbar), not a creative act (insha).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof lies in the encumbrance of capital: both the mother and child are tied to the capital until it is fully recovered. Profit does not appear in assets that are equally encumbered.
The text states: 'كل واحد من الجارية وولدها مشغول برأس المال، فلا يظهر الربح فيه'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The legal link is that ownership of the child (necessary for paternity claim) requires the child to be part of profit. Since profit is not realized until capital is recovered, ownership is absent, blocking the claim.
Contrast with emancipation: emancipation is an act of creation and cannot be retroactively validated.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Zufar argues that profit appears immediately because no single asset has priority over another in representing capital.
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) oppose this, insisting on capital recovery first.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position is the majority view, as stated in the text: 'عندنا، خلافاً لزفر'.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of precaution in protecting capital.

Source Text

(طائي) فإن قبض الألف إلخ : أي فإن قبض رب المال الألف، ضمن المدعي أي المضارب نصف قيمتها أي نصف قيمة الجارية، وإنما كان كذلك؛ لأن دعوة المضارب وقعت صحيحة ظاهراً؛ لأنه يحمل على أنه ولده من النكاح بأن ولدها وإنما الثابت له مجرد حق التصرف، فلا تنفذ دعوته. فإذا زادت قيمة الغلام وصارت ألفاً وخمس مائة ظهر الربح فيه في ذلك الوقت، ويملك المضارب منه نصف الزيادة، فنفذت دعوته السابقة فيه؛ لوجود شرطها، وهو الملك، بخلاف ما إذا أعتق الولد، ثم ظهرت الزيادة، حيث لا ينفذ إعتاقه السابق؛ لأن الإعتاق إنشاء، فإذا بطل؛ لعدم الملك، لا ينفذ إعتاقه بعده؛ لحدوثه، فأما الدعوة فإخبار، فإذا أو فى حق غيره، فهو باق في حق نفسه، فإذا هلكه بعد ذلك نفذت دعوته فيه، كما إذا أقر بحرية عبد لغيره يرد ،إقراره، فإذا ملكه بعد ذلك صار حرا، ولو اعتق عبد الغير ثم ملكه لا ينفذ عتقه لما قلنا، فإذا نفذت دعوته صار الغلام ابنا له وعتق بقدر نصيبه منه وهو ربعه ولم يضمن المضارب حصة رب المال من الولد؛ لأن العتق ثبت بالملك والنسب، فصارت العلة ذات ،وجهين والملك آخرهما، فيضاف الحكم وهو العتق إليه؛ لأن الحكم يضاف إلى الوصف الأخير، ولا صنع وظهر أن الأم كلها ربح لفراغها عن رأس المال، فكانت بينهما نصفين، ونفذ فيها دعوة المضارب، وصارت كلها أم ولد له ويجب نصف قيمتها لرب المال موسرا كان أو معسرا؛ لأنه ضمان التملك، وهو لا يختلف بالإعسار For the owner of capital, regarding the thousand and its quarter, or he emancipates it, then if he receives the thousand, the claimant (the mudarib) is liable for half its value, that is two hundred and fifty. Meaning the owner of capital claims paternity even if insolvent, only in the value of the slave woman and her child. And if profit appears in the child alone without the mother, the owner of capital is liable for what pertains to the mudarib in the value of the child only, so contemplate; for I have not seen anyone point this out. (Commentary: 'or he emancipates' means the owner of capital, so he has the option. 'Then if he receives the thousand etc.' means if the owner of capital receives the thousand, the claimant, i.e., the mudarib, is liable for half its value, i.e., half the value of the slave girl. This is because the mudarib's claim is apparently valid; it is assumed that the child is from marriage, that the seller married her to him and then sold her while pregnant, acting on the presumption of integrity. But this claim is not executed due to lack of ownership, which is a condition; since both the slave girl and her child are encumbered by the capital, profit does not appear in them. According to us, when mudarabah assets become different types each exceeding capital, profit does not appear, contrary to Zufar. Thus the mudarib has no share in the mother or child, only the right of disposal, so his claim is not executed. If the child's value increases to fifteen hundred, profit appears at that time, and the mudarib owns half the increase, so his previous claim is executed due to the existence of ownership, unlike if he had emancipated the child then the increase appeared, where his previous emancipation would not be executed because emancipation is a creation and if it fails due to lack of ownership, it does not execute later; but a claim is a report, so if it fulfills another's right, it remains for his own right. If he later owns it, his claim is executed, as if he acknowledges the freedom of another's slave—his acknowledgment is rejected, but if he later owns it, the slave becomes free. If he emancipates another's slave then owns it, his emancipation does not execute. When his claim executes, the child becomes his son and becomes free to the extent of his share, which is a quarter, and the mudarib does not guarantee the owner's share of the child, because emancipation is established by both ownership and lineage, and ownership is the later cause, so the ruling is attributed to the later description. The mudarib had no part in emancipation, so he is not liable for guarantee due to lack of transgression; guarantee for emancipation is only due to transgression. Thus the owner of capital has the option: if he wishes, he emancipates his share of the child, or if he wishes, he seeks compensation. If he receives a thousand dirhams from him, he recovers his capital, and it appears that the entire mother is profit, so it is divided half and half between them, and the mudarib's claim in her executes, and she becomes an umm walad for him, and half her value is obligatory for the owner of capital, whether he is wealthy or insolvent, because it is a guarantee of ownership, which does not differ with insolvency or wealth, and does not depend on transgression, unlike guarantee of emancipation, which is guarantee of corruption, so it is not obligatory without transgression nor on an insolvent person. The condition of wealth in the book is to indicate that guarantee is not obligatory on anyone; since it is not obligatory on the wealthy, it is even less so on the insolvent. (Ayni, Fath, according to need with a slight increase) 'Then if he receives etc.' means if the owner of capital receives the thousand from the child, the claimant, the mudarib, is liable for half its value, based on the previous statement that he has recovered his capital, so it appears the entire mother is profit, and she is divided half and half.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It deals with a complex scenario in mudarabah where the mudarib (agent) claims paternity of a child born to a slave woman purchased with mudarabah capital. The ruling explains that initially the mudarib's claim is not effective because the capital has not been recovered and ownership is not established. However, if the value of the child increases to the point where profit appears, the mudarib's claim becomes valid. The text distinguishes between a claim of paternity and an act of emancipation; the former is merely informational and can take effect later when ownership is acquired, while the latter is a creative act that cannot be retroactively validated. The owner of capital has options regarding his share of the child. Ultimately, the passage clarifies the rules of liability and profit distribution in such mixed cases.

Key Terms
مضاربة (muḍārabah): A partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor for profit-sharing.
رب المال (rabb al-māl): The owner of the capital in a mudarabah contract.
المضارب (al-muḍārib): The agent in a mudarabah who manages the capital with labor.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has given birth to her master's child, thereby gaining certain rights including eventual freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, why is a mudarib's claim of paternity over a child not immediately executable?
A) Because the child is considered part of the capital until the mother is sold.
B) Because the assets are encumbered by capital, and profit does not appear until capital is recovered.
C) Because the mudarib must have explicit permission from the capital owner.
D) Because the claim is invalid if the child is born after the contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi mudarabah, a mudarib's claim of paternity becomes effective only when capital is recovered, because profit is not realized until then.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

Mudarib Sub-Contracting Mudarabah: Permission and Liability
Rulings on a mudarib giving mudarabah to another without permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mudarib's liability when sub-contracting without permission and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Zufar.

Core Idea
A mudarib who sub-contracts without permission is not liable until the second mudarib works.

Mudarabah is a partnership in profit where one party provides capital and the other provides labor. A fundamental rule is that the mudarib (worker) cannot delegate his role without the owner's permission. But what if he does — and the capital is lost before the second mudarib does any work? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on liability in such cases, including an important intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a mudarib giving capital to another person for mudarabah without the owner's permission. The ruling: the first mudarib is not liable merely by handing over the capital.
This contrasts with Zufar's view that payment alone triggers liability.
2
Understand the condition for liability
Liability arises only when the second mudarib actually works. Until work begins, the arrangement is treated like a deposit (wadi'ah).
The work makes it a valid mudarabah, which the first mudarib cannot do without authorization.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Three positions exist: apparent Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn say liability upon work; Zufar says upon payment; a third transmission says upon profit.
The relied-upon position is liability upon work.
4
Apply to a scenario
If the second mudarib receives capital but loses it before working, the first mudarib is not liable. If the second works and then loses it, the first is liable.
This practical difference is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he gives with permission, (the share is) a third, from the owner of capital merely by payment. And half its value is obligatory for the owner of capital. He said in al-Fath: 'The condition that the owner of capital receives the thousand from the child is so that the slave girl becomes umm walad for the mudarib; because she is encumbered by capital, so when he receives it from the child, she is freed from capital, so she becomes entirely (available) and the mudarib's ownership appears in her, so she becomes his umm walad.' … He said in al-Zayla'i: 'That which is received is from his capital, so it is more appropriate to make it from capital, because capital takes precedence over profit; no profit is confirmed for them except after the capital is returned to the owner.' (Maskin: 'until the second works' because by work it becomes clear that it is mudarabah and he does not own it, so he is liable, except if the second is invalid as the commentator will mention, then no liability even if profit, but the second has his equivalent wage from the first mudarib, and the first has the stipulated profit. An analog in the book is if he said: 'Whatever of this is provision, it is between us half and half' or 'Take this capital as mudarabah for half.') Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter on a mudarib giving mudarabah to another person. It presents the ruling that if the mudarib sub-contracts without permission from the owner of capital, he is not liable for the money just by handing it over; liability arises only when the second mudarib actually works. This is the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and is the apparent ruling from Abu Hanifa. Zufar holds that liability is immediate upon payment. There is also a transmission from al-Hasan that liability only arises after profit. The text distinguishes between valid and invalid mudarabah. If the second mudarabah is invalid, the first mudarib is not liable; the second gets a fair wage. Then the case with permission is discussed: the first mudarib may retain his share of profit, and the sub-contracting is analogized to a common laborer hiring a substitute.

Key Terms
بإذن (bi-idhn): With permission; the owner of capital's permission for the mudarib to sub-contract.
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): The fair market wage for a similar service; given to the second mudarib if the sub-contract is invalid.
ضمان (daman): Liability; here it refers to the responsibility of the first mudarib to compensate the capital owner if the capital is lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when does a mudarib become liable for sub-contracting without permission?
A) Immediately upon handing over the capital.
B) Only after the second mudarib works.
C) Only after profit is realized.
D) He is never liable regardless.

Key Takeaway
The key point: a mudarib who sub-contracts without permission is only liable once the second mudarib actually works, as the contract is not established as mudarabah until then.

When Does a Mudarib Become Liable for Unauthorized Sub-Contracting?
Ruling on mudarib sub-contracting without owner's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on when a mudarib becomes liable for unauthorized sub-contracting and explain the points of disagreement within the school.

Core Idea
Unauthorized sub-contracting by a mudarib triggers liability only when the second mudarib begins work, not upon mere payment.

This lesson addresses a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the liability (daman) of a mudarib who hands over the capital to another mudarib without the owner's permission. While the majority holds that liability arises only after the second mudarib works, other scholars like Zufar and a rare narration from Abu Yusuf argue for immediate liability upon payment. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi emphasis on actual disposition in agency contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Majority Position
The matn and the commentary state that the first mudarib is not liable merely by handing over the capital; he becomes liable only when the second mudarib actually starts working. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
2
Recognize the Opponent's View (Zufar and a Narration)
Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl holds that liability is established by the act of payment alone, regardless of whether the second mudarib works. A similar view is attributed to a narration from Abu Yusuf.
Listen for the opposing voice in the commentary.
3
Examine the Opponent's Proof
Zufar's reasoning is that handing over the capital is a disposition that immediately transfers control, making the first mudarib responsible for any subsequent loss.
Understand the basis of the difference: immediate vs. conditional liability.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi majority responds that mere payment does not finalize the sub-contract; the second mudarib can still return the capital. The work confirms the mudaraba and shows that the first mudarib effectively delegated his authority, which he was not entitled to do without permission.
Thus, liability is tied to the confirmation of the sub-contract.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The practical difference: if the capital is lost before the second mudarib begins work, the first mudarib is not liable according to the majority, but is liable according to Zufar.
This affects real cases where the capital is destroyed or stolen before any trading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المضارب يضارب الذي يضارب من غيره
سم
فإن ضارب المضارب بلا إذن لم يضمن ما لم يعمل الثاني فإن دفع باذن بالثلث من رب المال بمجرد الدفع
فإن ضارب إلخ : أي فإن ضارب المضارب بلا إذن رب المال لم يضمن بمجرد الدفع ما لم يعمل المضارب الثاني مطلقاً، سواء ربح أو لم يربح، وهذا عندهما، وهو ظاهر الرواية عن أبي حنيفة له، وقال زفر: يضمن بالدفع عمل أو لم يعمل، وهو رواية عن أ أبي يوسف بالله، وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة: لا يضمن بالدفع حتى يربح، حتى لو هلك المال قبل ظهور الربح لا يضمن كلاهما، فإذا ربح ضمن الأول لرب المال، هذا إذا كانت المضاربة صحيحة، فإن كانت فاسدة لا يضمن الأول وإن عمل الثاني. (مسكين) ما لم يعمل الثاني إلخ لأن بالعمل تبين أنه مضاربة وهو لا يملكها، فيضمن إلا إذا كانت الثانية فاسدة كما سيذكره الشارح، فلا ضمان وإن ربح، بل للثاني أجر مثله على المضارب الأول، وللأول الربح المشروط "در". (فتح)
Chapter of the mudarib sub-contracting with another mudarib. If the mudarib sub-contracts without permission, he does not guarantee until the second works; if he gives with permission, one-third, from the capital owner by mere payment. The explanation: i.e., if the mudarib sub-contracts without the capital owner's permission, he does not guarantee by mere payment until the second mudarib works absolutely, whether profit or no profit. This is according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and is the apparent transmission from Abu Hanifa. Zufar said: he guarantees by payment whether he works or not; and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf. In the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa: he does not guarantee by payment until profit appears, so if the capital perished before profit appears, neither guarantees; when profit appears, the first guarantees to the capital owner. This is if the mudaraba is valid; if it is invalid, the first does not guarantee even if the second works. (Miskin) "Until the second works" because by working it becomes clear that it is a mudaraba and he does not own it, so he guarantees, except if the second is invalid as the commentator will mention, then no guarantee even if profit, rather the second has his equivalent wage from the first mudarib, and the first has the stipulated profit. (Fath)
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings on a mudarib (investment agent) giving the capital to another mudarib to work with. It is a dispute within the Hanafi school. The passage first states the matn: if the mudarib sub-contracts without permission, he is not liable until the second mudarib actually works. The commentary clarifies: 'without permission' means without the capital owner's permission. The first mudarib is not liable by merely handing over the capital; liability only arises when the second mudarib begins work. This is the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and it is also the apparent transmission from Abu Hanifa. Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl differed, holding that liability is established by the act of handing over, regardless of work. A narration from Abu Yusuf agrees with Zufar. A narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa states that liability only arises after profit appears; if the capital is lost before any profit, neither the first nor the second mudarib is liable. The commentary then distinguishes between valid and invalid sub-mudaraba: if the second mudaraba is invalid, the first mudarib is not liable even if the second works; instead, the second gets an equivalent wage, and the first takes the stipulated profit. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the one in the matn: liability only after work.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The investment agent who receives capital for mudaraba and may sub-contract if permitted.
ضمان (daman): Liability for the capital; here, the first mudarib becomes responsible to replace the capital if lost due to unauthorized sub-contracting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when does the first mudarib become liable for the capital if he sub-contracts without the owner's permission?
A) Immediately upon handing over the money to the second mudarib.
B) Only after the second mudarib works, regardless of profit or loss.
C) Only after profit is earned by the second mudarib.
D) Only if the second mudarib also sub-contracts further.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds that unauthorized sub-contracting creates liability only when the second mudarib begins work, not upon mere payment or profit.

Mudarib's Slave-Girl Becoming Umm Walad
Condition for a mudarib's slave-girl to become umm walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a mudarib's slave-girl to become umm walad and explain the underlying principle that capital precedes profit.

Core Idea
A mudarib's slave-girl only becomes umm walad after the capital debt is cleared, because capital precedes profit.

In this passage, the Hanafi jurists clarify a subtle point in mudaraba: a mudarib who owns a slave-girl and has a child with her cannot immediately claim her as umm walad. Her status is blocked by the debt owed to the capital owner. We will explore the reasoning and the principle that capital takes priority over profit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Scenario
Determine who the mudarib is, what the capital owner claims, and the status of the slave-girl. The passage begins with 'half its value is obligatory for the capital owner' — this sets up the debt.
The slave-girl is 'occupied by the capital' meaning she serves as collateral for the debt.
2
Isolate the Condition for Umm Walad
Find the exact condition stated: the capital owner must receive the thousand from the boy (or equivalent) for the slave-girl to become umm walad. This is the payment of capital.
The mudarib's full ownership is contingent on clearing the capital debt.
3
Analyze the Objection and Response
The passage raises an objection: why not treat the received amount as profit? The response is that capital precedes profit; profit is only legitimate after capital is safe. Work through this logical refutation.
This is a classic istihsan argument based on the priority of capital.
4
Derive the Underlying Principle
Extract the general rule: 'Capital takes precedence over profit; nothing of profit is secure until capital is safe.' This is the operative principle in mudaraba.
This principle is from al-Fath and al-Zayla'i, cited as authoritative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب نصف قيمتها لرب المال، قال في "الفتح": وإنما شرط قبض رب المال الألف من الغلام، حتى تصير الجارية أم ولد للمضارب؛ لأنها مشغولة برأس المال، فإذا قبضه من الغلام فرغت عن رأس المال، فصارت كلها فظهر فيها ملك المضارب فصارت أم ولد له رمز الحقائق : ٢٤٣/٢] فإن قيل : لم لا يجعل المقبوض من ربح الولد من الربح وهو ممكن بأن يجعل الولد كله ربحاً وهي مشغولة برأس المال على حالها؟ قلنا: المقبوض من رأس ماله، فكان أولى يجعله من رأس المال؛ لأن رأس المال مقدم على الربح؛ إذ لا يسلم لهما شيء من الربح إلا بعد سلامة رأس المال لرب المال، كذا في "الزيلعي".
And half its value is obligatory for the capital owner. He said in al-Fath: The condition of the capital owner taking the thousand from the boy is so that the slave-girl becomes umm walad for the mudarib; because she is occupied by the capital. When he takes it from the boy, she becomes free from the capital, so she becomes entirely [owned], and the mudarib's ownership appears in her, so she becomes his umm walad. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:243) If it is said: Why not consider the received amount from the profit of the child as profit? It is possible by making the entire child profit while she remains occupied by the capital. We reply: The received amount is from his capital, so it is more appropriate to make it from the capital, because capital precedes profit; nothing of profit is secure for them until the capital is safe for the owner. So it is in al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal status of a slave-girl owned by a mudarib who becomes pregnant by him (umm walad). The ruling is that she does not become umm walad until the mudarib first pays off the capital he owes to the capital owner from the boy's price (or equivalent). The reasoning is that the slave-girl is 'occupied' by the capital owed; until that debt is cleared, the mudarib's ownership over her is incomplete. Once he pays the capital, his full ownership emerges, and she can be an umm walad. The passage then addresses a potential objection: why not consider the payment as coming from the profit of the child (i.e., treat the child as profit)? The answer is that capital takes precedence over profit; the profit is only legitimate after the capital is fully repaid. Therefore, the payment must be allocated to capital first. This ruling is from al-Fath and al-Zayla'i.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who bears a child to her owner; she becomes free upon her owner's death and cannot be sold.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The principal capital invested in the mudaraba; it must be preserved before any profit distribution.
مضارب (mudarib): The entrepreneur or manager in a mudaraba contract who invests the capital provided by the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the mudarib pay the capital owner first before his slave-girl can become umm walad?
A) Because the slave-girl is owned by the capital owner until the debt is paid.
B) Because the capital is more important than profit, and profit cannot be taken until capital is safe.
C) Because the mudarib cannot own any slave-girl while in a mudaraba contract.
D) Because the slave-girl must be freed first by the capital owner.

Key Takeaway
A mudarib's slave-girl becomes umm walad only after the capital debt is fully repaid, because capital takes precedence over profit.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
