Dismissal of Mudarib and Sale of Goods After Dismissal
Ruling on dismissal of mudarib and handling of goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for dismissal of a mudarib and explain his right to sell goods after dismissal, distinguishing between qiyas and istihsan regarding currency type.

Core Idea
Dismissal of the mudarib is effective only upon his knowledge; after dismissal, he may sell goods to realize profit but cannot use the proceeds.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and al-Zaylai, we learn the Hanafi ruling on the dismissal (azl) of the mudarib (investment agent) by the capital owner. The key principle is that dismissal requires the mudarib's knowledge to take effect. Even after dismissal, if the capital is in the form of goods, the mudarib retains the right to sell them in order to realize his share of profit. However, his authority ends once the goods are converted to cash. This lesson explores the reasoning behind these rules and the difference between qiyas and istihsan when the capital is in a different currency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for dismissal
The passage states that the mudarib is dismissed only if he knows of the dismissal. Note: This is unlike general agency (wakala) where legal dismissal suffices without knowledge.
2
Determine what constitutes knowledge
Knowledge is established by the testimony of two men, or one just person if the mudarib is a volunteer (fuduli), otherwise by a report of a discerning person. Note: This ensures the mudarib is reliably informed.
3
Understand the mudarib's right to sell goods after dismissal
After dismissal, if the capital is goods, the mudarib may sell them, even on credit, even if the owner forbids. Note: This right is based on the mudarib's claim to profit, which only appears through sale.
4
Recognize the limits after sale and the istihsan for currency conversion
After the sale, the mudarib cannot dispose of the price. If the capital was in a different currency, by qiyas he cannot sell to obtain the capital type, but by istihsan he may, because his obligation is to return the like. Note: Istihsan overrides qiyas to facilitate the mudarib's obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينعزل إلخ: أي ينعزل المضارب بعزل رب المال إن علم به؛ لأنه وكيل وإن لم يعلم لا، والمراد بالعلم ما خبر رجلين مطلقاً، أو واحد عدل إن كان فضولياً، وإلا فخبر مميز. [البحر الرائق: ٣٨٥/٧] من إن علم إلخ: لا فرق في اشتراط العلم بالعزل بين العزل الحكمي وغيره في المضاربة، بخلاف الوكيل، فإنه ينعزل في الحكمي وإن لم يعلم كذا في "البحر" و"الزيلعي" . (فتح) وإن علم إلخ أي وإن علم المضارب بعزله والمال عروض باعها ولو نسيئة ولو نهاه ولا يملك فسخها في هذه الحالة ولا تخصيص الإذن؛ لأنه عزل من وجه، بخلاف أحد الشريكين إذا فسخها ومالها أمتعة، ولا يمنع المضارب العزل عن بيعها؛ لأن له حقاً في الربح، ولا يظهر إلا بالنقد، فثبت له حق البيع؛ ليظهر ذلك، وموته وارتداده مع اللحوق وجنونه مطبقاً والمال عروض كعزله والمال عروض كذا في "الزيلعي [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٦/٢] ثم لا يتصرف إلخ: أي إذا باع العروض فلا يتصرف بعد ذلك في ثمنها؛ لأن البيع بعد العزل كان للضرورة، فلم يبق بعد النقد، ولو عزله والمال نقود لكن من خلاف جنس رأس المال ليس له أن يبيعه بجنس رأس المال قياساً؛ لأن النقدين جنس واحد من حيث الثمنية، وفي الاستحسان له أن يبيعه بجنس رأس المال؛ لأن الواجب عليه رد مثل رأس المال، وإنما يتحقق ذلك برد جنسه [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢]
And he is dismissed etc.: meaning the mudarib is dismissed by the dismissal of the capital owner if he knows of it, because he is an agent, and if he does not know, he is not dismissed. And by knowledge is meant the report of two men absolutely, or one just person if he is a volunteer (fuduli), otherwise the report of a discerning person. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:385] From 'if he knows' etc.: There is no difference in stipulating knowledge of dismissal between legal dismissal and other in mudarabah, contrary to the agent (wakil), as he is dismissed legally even if he does not know. Thus in al-Bahr and al-Zaylai. (Fath) And if he knows etc.: meaning if the mudarib knows of his dismissal and the capital is goods, he sells them, even on credit, even if the owner forbade him; and he does not own the right to cancel (faskh) in this situation, nor to specify the permission, because it is a dismissal from one aspect, contrary to one of the two partners if he cancels (the shirkah) and their capital is merchandise; and the dismissal does not prevent the mudarib from selling them, because he has a right in the profit, and it does not appear except through cash (naqd), so his right to sell is established to make it appear. And his death, apostasy with joining, and continuous insanity while the capital is goods are like his dismissal while the capital is goods. Thus in al-Zaylai [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:246]. Then he does not dispose etc.: meaning if he sells the goods, he does not thereafter dispose of their price, because the sale after dismissal was for necessity, so it does not remain after the cash. And if he dismissed him while the capital is cash but of a different type from the capital, he may not sell it for the capital type by qiyas because the two types are in the same genus in terms of serving as price; but by istihsan he may sell it for the capital type because what is obligatory on him is to return the like of the capital, and that is only realized by returning its genus. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:247]
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage addresses the dismissal (azl) of the mudarib by the capital owner and its consequences. The matn states that dismissal only takes effect if the mudarib knows of it, because mudarabah is a form of agency, which requires knowledge for dismissal. The commentator specifies that knowledge can be established by the testimony of two men or one just person if the mudarib is a volunteer (i.e., acting without prior request). If the mudarib is not a volunteer, a report from a discerning person suffices. Importantly, unlike general agency, in mudarabah there is no difference between legal (hukmi) dismissal and explicit dismissal: both require knowledge. After dismissal, if the capital is in the form of goods (urud), the mudarib has the right to sell them, even on credit and even if the owner explicitly forbids it. This is because the mudarib has a claim to profit, which only becomes apparent upon conversion to cash. Therefore, he is entitled to sell to realize the profit. This right extends to cases of the mudarib's death, apostasy, or continuous insanity when the capital is goods. However, after the sale, the mudarib has no further authority over the sale price; he cannot trade with it. If the capital is cash of a different type than the original capital (e.g., if capital was dirhams and the goods are dinars), by qiyas he cannot sell to obtain the identical type (since both are cash), but by istihsan (juristic preference) he can, because his obligation is to return the like of the capital, which is only possible by returning the same type.

Key Terms
عزل (ʿazl): Dismissal: the act of the capital owner removing the mudarib from his authority to trade with the capital.
عروض (ʿurūḍ): Goods, merchandise: non-cash assets that are traded. In this context, the capital of the mudarabah is in the form of tangible goods rather than money.
ثمن (thaman): Price, sale proceeds: the cash received from selling goods. After dismissal, the mudarib cannot dispose of this price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After the mudarib is dismissed and the capital is in goods, what is his authority regarding the goods?
A) He can sell the goods but cannot use the sale proceeds.
B) He must return the goods immediately without selling.
C) He can continue trading freely as before.
D) He can sell only with the owner's explicit permission.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's dismissal requires his knowledge; after dismissal, he retains the right to sell goods to realize profit but cannot trade with the proceeds.

Forcing the Mudarib to Collect Debts Upon Separation
Ruling on forcing mudarib to collect debts with proof and exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which the mudarib is forced to collect debts and explain the legal analogy (qiyas to ajir).

Core Idea
The mudarib is forced to collect debts only if profit exists; otherwise he is a volunteer not compelled.

When a mudarabah ends with debts and profit, the mudarib must complete his work by collecting those debts. This obligation stems from his resemblance to an employee whose wage (profit) has been secured. If there is no profit, he is merely a volunteer and cannot be forced; instead, he must appoint the capital owner to collect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The mudarib is forced (yujbar) to collect debts. Determine the hukm level: wajib.
The ruling is conditional on the presence of profit.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas: the mudarib is like an ajir (employee) because profit is his wage; once the wage is due, he must complete the work.
This is a qiyas jaliy.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The analogy rests on the illa: both receive a benefit (wage/profit) for their work. Therefore, like an employee, the mudarib is obliged to perform the tasks that realise his profit.
The text states 'because he is like an ajir'.
4
Note the exception
If there is no profit, the mudarib is a pure agent (wakil) and volunteer; no compulsion applies. Instead, he is ordered to appoint the owner as his agent to safeguard the owner's rights.
This prevents the owner's right from being lost.
5
Apply the rule to general agents
The same principle applies to any agent for sale (wakil bil bay') or consignee (mustabda'): they are not forced to litigate but must appoint the owner.
This extends the ruling by qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو افترقا إلخ: أي المضارب ورب المال والحال أن في المال ديونا وربحاً، أجبر المضارب على اقتضاء الديون؛ لأنه كالأجير، والربح كالأجرة له، وقد سلم له ذلك، فيجبر على إتمام عمله [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] من غير وإلا لا يلزمه إلخ: أي وإن لم يكن في المال ربح لا يلزمه الاقتضاء؛ لأنه وكيل محض، وهو متبرع، فلا . جبر على المتبرع على إيفاء ما تبرع به. رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] ويوكل إلخ : أي يوكل المضارب المالك أي رب المال عليه أي على الاقتضاء؛ لأن حقوق العقد تتعلق بالعاقد، ورب المال ليس بعاقد، فلا يتمكن من المطالبة إلا بالتوكيل فيؤمر بالتوكيل كي لا يضيع حقه، وعلى هذا كل وكيل بالبيع وكل مستبضع إذا امتنع من التقاضي لا يجبر عليه، ولكن يجبر على أن يجعل صاحب المال وكيلاً كي لا يضيع حقه. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] (فتح) والسمسار إلخ: قال في "البحر الرائق: وهو بكسر الأول المتوسط بين البائع والمشتري، وجمعه سماسرة يبيع ويشتري للناس بأجر أن يستأجر، ولو استؤجر على البيع والشراء لا يجوز؛ لعدم قدرته عليه، والحيلة في جوازها أن يستأجره يوماً للخدمة، فيستعمله في البيع والشراء إلى آخر المدة، ولو عمل من غير شرط، وأعطاه شيئاً لا بأس به، و به جرت العادة، وإنما أجبر على طلب الثمن من المشتري واستيفائه؛ لأنه جملة عمله. [٣٨٦/٧] من يجبر إلخ لأنه يبيع ويشتري للناس عادة بأجرة، فجعل ذلك بمنزلة الإجارة الصحيحة بحكم العادة، فيجب عليه التقاضي والاستيفاء؛ لأنه وصل إليه بدل ،عمله فصار كالمضارب إذا كان في المال تبيين الحقائق: ٥٤٤/٥] ربح. وما هلك إلخ أي كل شيء هلك من مال المضاربة فمن الربح أي فيجعل منه ؛ لأنه تابع، ورأس المال أصل، فيصرف الهلاك إلى التابع كما في العفو في الزكاة. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] وقال في "الفتح": والقول للشريك والمضارب في مقدار الربح والخسران مع يمينه ولا يلزمه أن يذكر الأمر مفصلاً، والقول قوله في الضياع والرد إلى الشريك، "نهر" في الشركة فإن زاد الهالك إلخ: فإن زاد الهالك على الربح حتى ينقص من رأس المال مثلاً: بأن كان رأس المال ألفاً والربح مائة، والهالك مائة وخمسون، لم يضمن المضارب؛ لأنه أمين، فلا يكون ضميناً. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] سواء كان من عمله أو لا. [البحر الرائق: ٣٨٦/٧]
And if they separate etc.: meaning the mudarib and the owner of capital, while there are debts and profit in the capital, the mudarib is forced to collect the debts, because he is like an employee (ajir), and the profit is like his wage, and it has been delivered to him, so he is forced to complete his work. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:247] Other than that, if not, it does not obligate him etc.: meaning if there is no profit in the capital, he is not obligated to collect, because he is a pure agent and a volunteer, and there is no compulsion on a volunteer to fulfill what he volunteered. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:247] And he appoints etc.: meaning the mudarib appoints the owner (i.e., the capital owner) over him (i.e., over collection), because the rights of the contract are attached to the contracting party, and the capital owner is not a contracting party, so he cannot claim except by appointment, so he is ordered to appoint so that his right is not lost. On this basis, every agent for sale and every mustabda' (one who sends goods for sale) if he refrains from litigation, he is not forced, but he is forced to make the owner of capital an agent so that his right is not lost. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:247] (Fath) And the samsar etc.: He said in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: It (samsar) is the middleman between seller and buyer, its plural is samasirah; they buy and sell for people for a wage; he is hired, and if he is hired for buying and selling, it is not permissible because he is unable to do so. The stratagem (hila) for its permissibility is that he hires him for a day for service, then he uses him for buying and selling until the end of the period; and if he works without a condition and gives him something, there is no harm, and custom has been established on this; and he is forced to demand the price from the buyer and collect it, because it is the sum of his work. [7:386] From 'he is forced' etc., because he usually buys and sells for people for a wage, so that is made like a valid lease by virtue of custom, so he must demand and collect because the substitute for his work has reached him, so he becomes like the mudarib when there is profit in the capital. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 5:544] And whatever perishes etc.: meaning everything that perishes from the mudarabah property is from the profit, i.e., it is made from it, because it is subordinate, and the capital is original, so loss is directed to the subordinate, as in the forgiveness in zakat. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:247] And it is said in al-Fath: The statement (i.e., the mudarib's statement) is accepted regarding the amount of profit and loss with his oath, and he is not required to mention the matter in detail; and his statement is accepted regarding loss and return to the partner. 'Nahr' in al-Shirkah: If the loss exceeds the profit until it reduces the capital, for example if the capital was 1,000 and the profit was 100 and the loss is 150, the mudarib is not liable because he is a trustee (amin), so he is not a guarantor. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:247] Whether it is from his action or not. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:386]
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct rulings that apply when the mudarabah is terminated or the parties separate. First, concerning debts: if there are debts owed to the mudarabah and there is also profit, the mudarib is forced to collect those debts. The reason given is that the mudarib is like an employee (ajir) and the profit is like his wage; since the wage has been designated to him, he must complete the work to obtain it. If there is no profit, the mudarib is a pure agent (wakil) and a volunteer, so he cannot be forced to collect. Instead, he is ordered to appoint the capital owner as his agent to collect, so that the owner's right is not lost. This applies to all agents and consignees who refuse litigation. Second, the broker (samsar): he is a middleman who buys and sells for people for a fee. Normally, hiring for sale/purchase is invalid due to uncertainty, but by custom, the arrangement is valid using a stratagem (hila): hire him for a day of service, then use him for trade. If he works without prior condition and receives something, it is acceptable by custom. He is forced to demand and collect the price because that is the sum of his work; he is like the mudarib when there is profit. Third, regarding losses: any loss in the mudarabah property is first deducted from the profit, because profit is subordinate to capital. If the loss exceeds the profit and eats into the capital, the mudarib is not liable (he is an amin, trustee). This applies whether the loss was due to his action or not. The mudarib's statement about the amount of profit or loss is accepted with his oath, and he does not need to provide details.

Key Terms
اقتضاء الديون (iqtiḍāʾ al-duyūn): Collection of debts: the act of demanding and receiving payment from debtors of the mudarabah.
أجير (ajīr): Employee: a person hired for a wage.
وكيل محض (wakīl maḥḍ): Pure agent: an agent acting without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the mudarib forced to collect debts upon separation?
A) Only if there is profit in the mudarabah.
B) Only if the owner demands it.
C) Always, regardless of profit.
D) Only if the debts are from a solvent debtor.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib must collect debts only when profit exists; otherwise he is a volunteer who must appoint the owner.

The Broker (Samsar) and Forced Collection of Price
Ruling on the broker's duty to collect price and the hila validating his work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the hila that validates the broker's engagement and state why he is forced to collect the price.

Core Idea
The broker is forced to collect the price because custom treats his engagement as a valid lease, and collection is the core of his work.

The broker (samsar) facilitates sales for a fee. Normally hiring for sale/purchase is invalid due to uncertainty, but Hanafi jurists provide a stratagem (hila) to make it permissible. Once engaged, the broker is compelled to demand and collect the price, as this is the essence of his work. Custom ('urf) elevates his status to that of a mudarib with profit.

Guided Path
1
Define the samsar
The samsar is a middleman between buyer and seller, paid a fee for his service.
The term 'broker' is used interchangeably.
2
Identify the problem of hiring for sale/purchase
Hiring someone specifically to buy or sell is invalid because the outcome is uncertain (gharar).
Standard lease requires specified work.
3
Explain the hila
The stratagem is to hire the broker for a day of general service, then use him for buying/selling during that period.
Alternatively, if he works without a prior condition and receives payment, it is acceptable by custom.
4
State the ruling of forced collection
Since custom validates the arrangement, the broker is forced to demand and collect the price from the buyer, because that is the sum of his work.
He becomes like the mudarib when profit exists.
5
Identify the dalil
The proof is custom ('urf) and analogy to mudarib with profit.
Custom is recognized as a source of law in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] وقال في "الفتح": والقول للشريك والمضارب في مقدار الربح والخسران مع يمينه ولا يلزمه أن يذكر الأمر مفصلاً، والقول قوله في الضياع والرد إلى الشريك، "نهر" في الشركة فإن زاد الهالك إلخ: فإن زاد الهالك على الربح حتى ينقص من رأس المال مثلاً: بأن كان رأس المال ألفاً والربح مائة، والهالك مائة وخمسون، لم يضمن المضارب؛ لأنه أمين، فلا يكون ضميناً. Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

Allocation of Loss in Mudarabah Property
Ruling on loss allocation and mudarib's non-liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that loss is first subtracted from profit, and state that the mudarib is not liable for losses as he is a trustee (amin).

Core Idea
Loss is first allocated to profit (subordinate) and then to capital; the mudarib is never liable as he is a trustee.

When the mudarabah property is lost or damaged, the loss must be assigned in a specific order: first from any existing profit, then from the capital. The mudarib is not liable for the loss, even if caused by his actions, because he is a trustee (amin). His statement about the amount of profit or loss is accepted with oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for loss allocation
Any loss in mudarabah property is first deducted from profit. Profit is subordinate to capital.
The analogy is to zakat where forgiveness applies to the subordinate.
2
Understand the rationale
Profit is like a branch; loss affects the branch before the root. Capital is the original investment.
The text cites the example of zakat where the waiver applies to the less important part.
3
Note the extent of loss
If the loss exceeds the profit and diminishes the capital, the mudarib is still not liable.
He is an amin (trustee), not a guarantor.
4
Acceptance of mudarib's statement
The mudarib's statement about the amount of profit or loss is accepted with oath; he does not need to provide detailed accounts.
This builds trust and avoids hardship.
5
Apply the rule to all losses
The ruling applies whether the loss resulted from the mudarib's action or an external cause.
The mudarib is not liable in either case.

Source Text

If a mudarabah suffers a loss of $1,500 when capital was $10,000 and profit $1,000, how is the loss allocated?
A) $1,500 from capital; mudarib liable.

Mudarib's Disposal of Sale Proceeds: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Explains the mudarib's limitations and the override by istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mudarib's disposal of sale proceeds and apply the principle of istihsan to override qiyas in mudarabah transactions.

Core Idea
Istihsan permits the mudarib to exchange sale proceeds for the capital currency even when qiyas forbids it.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq examines the limits of a mudarib's authority after selling the capital. It contrasts two scenarios and presents the Hanafi reliance on istihsan, demonstrating a key methodological principle where juristic preference overrides strict analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two distinct cases: (1) when the capital was goods and the mudarib sold them, he cannot further dispose of the price; (2) when the capital was cash of a different currency and the mudarib isolated the price, qiyas forbids exchanging it for the capital's currency, but istihsan permits it.
Focus on the second scenario for the qiyas vs. istihsan contrast.
2
Understand the qiyas reasoning
Qiyas analogizes the two currencies: since both are thaman (price), they are considered a single genus, so exchanging one thaman for another is akin to selling price for price, which is impermissible. Thus, the mudarib cannot sell the price for the capital currency.
The underlying illa (effective cause) is that both are thaman; the hukm is prohibition.
3
Understand the istihsan reasoning
Istihsan overrides qiyas because the mudarib's duty is to return the equivalent of the capital. Returning the same type (jins) is necessary to fulfill this obligation. Custom and practical need justify the exception.
Istihsan is a source of law in the Hanafi school, used here to permit what qiyas forbids.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the istihsan ruling: the mudarib may sell the price for the capital currency. This is the operative rule for fatwa.
Ramz al-Haqa'iq confirms this as the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المضارب يضارب من غيره والمال عروض باعها، ثم لا يتصرف في ثمنها. ثم لا يتصرف إلخ: أي إذا باع العروض فلا يتصرف بعد ذلك في ثمنها؛ لأن البيع بعد العزل كان للضرورة، فلم يبق بعد النقد، ولو عزله والمال نقود لكن من خلاف جنس رأس المال ليس له أن يبيعه بجنس رأس المال قياساً؛ لأن النقدين جنس واحد من حيث الثمنية، وفي الاستحسان له أن يبيعه بجنس رأس المال؛ لأن الواجب عليه رد مثل رأس المال، وإنما يتحقق ذلك برد جنسه [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢]
The mudarib enters into mudarabah with others, and the capital is goods which he sold, then he does not dispose of their price. 'Then he does not dispose etc.': i.e., if he sold the goods, he does not thereafter dispose of their price, because the sale after isolation was for necessity, so after payment nothing remains. And if he isolated it and the capital was cash but of a different type from the capital, he may not sell it for the same type as the capital by qiyas, because the two currencies are one type in terms of being a price. But by istihsan, he may sell it for the same type as the capital, because the obligation upon him is to return the equivalent of the capital, and that is only realized by returning its type. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 247/2]
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. When a mudarib receives goods as capital (ʿurūḍ) and sells them, he is not permitted to dispose of the sale price (thaman). The commentary explains the reason: after the sale, the price becomes the new capital and the mudarib's authority ends. However, if the capital was cash (nuqud) but of a different currency, qiyas would forbid selling the price for the same currency as the capital because both are price. But the Hanafi school adopts istihsan: the mudarib may sell the price for the same currency to fulfill his duty of returning the equivalent of the capital. This is a classic example of istihsan overriding qiyas due to practical need. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the istihsan.

Key Terms
عروض (ʿurūḍ): Goods, commodities other than cash used as capital in mudarabah.
ثمن (thaman): Price or sale proceeds received from selling the goods.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference overriding qiyas based on necessity or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling by qiyas regarding the mudarib selling the price for the same currency as capital?
A) Permissible
B) Not permissible
C) Obligatory
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to permit the mudarib to exchange sale proceeds for the capital currency, overriding the qiyas prohibition.

Compelling the Mudarib and Broker to Collect Debts
Fiqh ruling with proof on compelling agents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mudarib and a broker are compelled to collect debts and explain the analogical reasoning (qiyas) used to derive these rulings.

Core Idea
A mudarib is compelled to collect debts only when profit exists; a broker is always compelled because custom treats him as hired.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains when a mudarib (working partner) and a broker (simsar) can be forced to demand payment after the mudarabah ends or after a sale. The ruling turns on the presence of profit for the mudarib and customary hiring for the broker. Let us unpack the conditions and the analogical reasoning provided by the commentators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the core ruling
The passage discusses two individuals: the mudarib (working partner in a mudarabah) and the simsar (broker). The core ruling: the mudarib is compelled to collect debts only when there is profit; otherwise he is not compelled but must appoint the owner. The broker is always compelled to collect debts.
The broker is treated as a hired worker due to custom.
2
Analyze the mudarib's compulsion condition
When the mudarabah ends and there are debts and profit, the mudarib is compelled to collect because he is like a hired worker (ajir) and the profit is like his wage. If there is no profit, he is a volunteer and cannot be forced.
The analogy: mudarib → ajir, profit → wage.
3
Understand the appointment mechanism when no profit
Even when not compelled, the mudarib must appoint the capital owner as his agent to collect debts, so that the owner's right is not lost. This applies to any agent or consignee who refuses to collect.
The owner cannot claim directly because he is not a party to the sales contract.
4
Examine the broker's compulsion and the custom-based hire
The broker is compelled to demand payment because custom treats him as a hired worker. Although direct hire for buying/selling is invalid due to uncertainty, a legal device (hilah) is used: hire him for a day's service and then employ him in trading. The compulsion arises because he has received customary payment.
The broker's position is analogous to the mudarib with profit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو افترقا وفي المال ديون وربح، أجبر المضارب على اقتضاء الديون، وإلا لا يلزمه الاقتضاء، ويوكل المالك عليه، والسمسار يجبر على التقاضي. ولو افترقا إلخ: أي المضارب ورب المال والحال أن في المال ديونا وربحاً، أجبر المضارب على اقتضاء الديون؛ لأنه كالأجير، والربح كالأجرة له، وقد سلم له ذلك، فيجبر على إتمام عمله [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] وإلا لا يلزمه إلخ: أي وإن لم يكن في المال ربح لا يلزمه الاقتضاء؛ لأنه وكيل محض، وهو متبرع، فلا جبر على المتبرع على إيفاء ما تبرع به. رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] ويوكل إلخ : أي يوكل المضارب المالك أي رب المال عليه أي على الاقتضاء؛ لأن حقوق العقد تتعلق بالعاقد، ورب المال ليس بعاقد، فلا يتمكن من المطالبة إلا بالتوكيل فيؤمر بالتوكيل كي لا يضيع حقه، وعلى هذا كل وكيل بالبيع وكل مستبضع إذا امتنع من التقاضي لا يجبر عليه، ولكن يجبر على أن يجعل صاحب المال وكيلاً كي لا يضيع حقه. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] (فتح) والسمسار إلخ: قال في "البحر الرائق: وهو بكسر الأول المتوسط بين البائع والمشتري، وجمعه سماسرة يبيع ويشتري للناس بأجر أن يستأجر، ولو استؤجر على البيع والشراء لا يجوز؛ لعدم قدرته عليه، والحيلة في جوازها أن يستأجره يوماً للخدمة، فيستعمله في البيع والشراء إلى آخر المدة، ولو عمل من غير شرط، وأعطاه شيئاً لا بأس به، و به جرت العادة، وإنما أجبر على طلب الثمن من المشتري واستيفائه؛ لأنه جملة عمله. [٣٨٦/٧] من يجبر إلخ لأنه يبيع ويشتري للناس عادة بأجرة، فجعل ذلك بمنزلة الإجارة الصحيحة بحكم العادة، فيجب عليه التقاضي والاستيفاء؛ لأنه وصل إليه بدل عمله فصار كالمضارب إذا كان في المال تبيين الحقائق: ٥٤٤/٥] ربح.
And if they separate while there are debts and profit in the capital, the mudarib is compelled to collect the debts. Otherwise, he is not obligated to collect, and he appoints the owner over it. And the broker is compelled to demand payment. 'And if they separate etc.': i.e., the mudarib and the owner of capital while there are debts and profit in the capital, the mudarib is compelled to collect the debts because he is like a hired worker, and the profit is like his wage, and it has been given to him, so he is compelled to complete his work [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 247/2]. 'Otherwise, he is not obligated etc.': i.e., if there is no profit in the capital, he is not obligated to collect because he is a pure agent and a volunteer, and a volunteer is not compelled to fulfill what he volunteered for. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 247/2] 'And he appoints etc.': i.e., the mudarib appoints the owner, i.e., the capital owner over the collection, because the rights of the contract are connected to the contracting party, and the capital owner is not a contracting party, so he cannot claim except through appointment. So he is ordered to appoint so that his right is not lost. And similarly, every agent for sale and every consignee if he refrains from demanding payment is not compelled, but he is compelled to make the owner an agent so that his right is not lost. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 247/2] (Fath) 'And the broker etc.': He said in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: He (the broker) is, with kasr of the first letter, the intermediary between seller and buyer; its plural is samasirah. He sells and buys for people for a wage. If he is hired to buy and sell, it is not permissible because of inability to do so. The legal device for its permissibility is that he hires him for a day for service, then employs him in buying and selling until the end of the period. And if he works without a condition and gives him something, it is fine, and custom has proceeded with it. He is compelled to demand the price from the buyer and collect it because that is the sum of his work. [386/7] 'Who is compelled etc.': Because he customarily sells and buys for people for a wage, so that is treated like a valid hire by virtue of custom, so he must demand and collect payment because he has received the equivalent of his work, so he becomes like the mudarib when there is profit in the capital. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 544/5]
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage addresses the obligations of the mudarib and the broker (simsar) regarding debt collection. The commentary explains the matn: when the mudarib and the capital owner separate (the mudarabah ends) and there are debts and profit, the mudarib is compelled to collect the debts because he is like an employee (ajir) and profit is his wage. If there is no profit, he is not compelled because he is a volunteer (mutabarri') - a volunteer cannot be forced to complete his gift. However, to protect the owner's rights, the mudarib is compelled to appoint the owner as his agent to collect. This applies to any agent or consignee. The broker, who customarily buys and sells for a fee, is compelled to collect payment because he is like a hired worker (by custom). The text also discusses the legal device (hilah) for hiring a broker: hire him for a day's service, then use him for buying and selling.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The working partner in a mudarabah contract who manages the capital.
سمسار (simsar): A broker who acts as intermediary between buyer and seller, usually for a fee.
تقاضي (taqāḍī): Demanding payment or collection of a debt.
أجير (ajīr): A hired worker, used as the basis for analogy (qiyas) for the mudarib.
عرف (ʿurf): Custom, used to validate the broker's hire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always
B) Only if there is profit
C) Only if the owner requests
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The compulsion to collect debts depends on whether the mudarib or broker has received compensation (profit or customary wage); otherwise, the agent is not forced but must enable the principal.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

Loss in Mudarabah: Profit Covers Loss and Return of Distributed Profit
Explains loss allocation in mudarabah and return of distributed profit with a legal device

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that loss in mudarabah is charged to profit first, and explain the conditions under which distributed profit must be returned to the capital owner.

Core Idea
Profit in mudarabah is subsidiary to capital: loss must first exhaust profit before touching capital, and distributed profit is revocable if capital is later lost.

Mudarabah (silent partnership) is a key Islamic finance contract. A common question is what happens when the business suffers a loss after profits have been distributed. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentaries clarifies that profit is subsidiary (tabi') to capital, so loss is first charged to profit. If profit was distributed and later capital is lost, the profit must be returned to restore the owner's capital, unless the contract was properly rescinded and renewed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle of Loss Allocation
The passage begins by stating that any loss from mudarabah capital is charged to profit. Recognize that profit is considered subsidiary (tabi') to the principal (asl).
This is the foundational rule: loss is first offset against profit before touching capital.
2
Examine the Condition for Returning Distributed Profit
The text specifies that if profit was distributed while the mudarabah contract remains in effect, and later all or part of the capital is lost, the parties must return the profit to restore the owner's capital.
The ruling applies only if the contract is still active (not rescinded).
3
Understand the Exception: Rescission and Renewal
If the parties rescinded the first mudarabah and then entered into a new one, loss of capital in the new contract does not affect the profit from the first. The profit is not returned.
The first contract is independent; its rulings are separate.
4
Learn the Legal Device (Hilah) to Protect Profit
To avoid the return of profit, the mudarib can return the capital to the owner, distribute the profit, and then the owner gives the capital back as a new mudarabah. This severs the link between the first profit and subsequent losses.
The device works even if the capital physically remains with the mudarib after rescission.
5
Review the Supporting Evidence
The passage cites a hadith from al-Zayla'i: 'The believer is like a merchant: he is not safe until his capital is safe.' This analogically supports the subsidiary nature of profit.
The hadith has zanni thubut, but is used as a supporting proof (isti'anah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما هلك من مال المضاربة فمن الربح فإن زاد الهالك على الربح لم يضمن المضارب، وإن قسم الربح وبقيت المضاربة ثم هلك المال أو بعضه ترادا الربح ليأخذ المالك رأس ماله وما فضل فهو بينهما، وإن نقص لم يضمن المضارب النقصان، وإن قسم الربح وفسخت ثم عقداها فهلك المال لم يترادًا الربح الأول. وما هلك إلخ أي كل شيء هلك من مال المضاربة فمن الربح أي فيجعل منه؛ لأنه تابع، ورأس المال أصل، فيصرف الهلاك إلى التابع كما في العفو في الزكاة. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] وقال في "الفتح": والقول للشريك والمضارب في مقدار الربح والخسران مع يمينه ولا يلزمه أن يذكر الأمر مفصلاً، والقول قوله في الضياع والرد إلى الشريك، "نهر" في الشركة فإن زاد الهالك إلخ: فإن زاد الهالك على الربح حتى ينقص من رأس المال مثلاً: بأن كان رأس المال ألفاً والربح مائة، والهالك مائة وخمسون، لم يضمن المضارب؛ لأنه أمين، فلا يكون ضميناً. (فتح) وما فضل إلخ: أي وما فضل من رأس المال بعد التراد، فهو بينهما أي بين المالك والمضارب؛ لأنه لأن رب المال لم يبق له حق بعد استيفاء ماله إلا في الربح. ربح؛ رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/٢] وإن نقص إلخ : أي وإن نقص من رأس المال بأن لم يكن الربح قدره، أي قدر الهالك لم يضمن المضارب أي النقصان؛ لما بينا أنه أمين فيه رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٨/٢] وإن قسم الربح إلخ أي وإن قسم الربح بين المضارب ورب المال، وفسخت المضاربة، ثم عقداها أي رب المال والمضارب المضاربة ثانياً، فهلك المال بعد ذلك كله أو بعضه لم يترادا أي المضارب ورب المال الربح الأول؛ لأن المضاربة الأولى قد انتهت بالفسخ، وثبوت الثانية بعقد جديد، فهلاك المال في الثانية لا يوجب انتقاص الأولى، فصار كما إذا دفع إليه مالاً آخر هذا. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٨/٢]. لم يترادا إلخ: لأن المضاربة الأولى قد انتهت بالفسخ وثبوت الثانية بعقد جديد، فهلاك المال في الثانية لا يوجب انتقاض الأولى، فصار كما إذا دفع إليه مالاً آخر، وهذه هي الحيلة فيما إذا خاف المضارب أن يسترد منه الربح بعد القسمة بسبب هلاك ما بقي في يده من رأس المال وصورة هذه أن يسلم المضارب رأس المال إلى رب المال ثم يقسمان الربح ثم يرد رب المال رأس المال إلى المضارب ويقول : اعمل على المضاربة فتكون تلك مضاربة مستقلة، كذا في "الزيلعي" لكن قوله : وصورة" هذه الحيلة أن يسلم المضارب رأس المال إلى رب المال" يوهم أنه شرط، وليس كذلك، حتى لو بقي المال بعد فسخ المضاربة في يد المضارب والمسألة بحالها لم يختلف الحكم. (فتح)
And whatever is lost from the capital of mudarabah is from the profit. If the loss exceeds the profit, the mudarib is not liable. And if they distributed the profit and the mudarabah remains, then the capital or part of it is lost, they return the profit so that the owner takes his capital, and what remains is between them. And if there is a deficiency, the mudarib is not liable for the deficiency. And if they distributed the profit and then rescinded and then renewed the mudarabah, then the capital is lost, they do not return the first profit. 'And whatever is lost etc.': i.e., everything that is lost from the capital of mudarabah is from the profit, i.e., it is made from it, because it is subsidiary, and the capital is the origin, so loss is attributed to the subsidiary as in the case of forgiveness in zakat. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 247/2] And he said in al-Fath: The statement of the partner and the mudarib regarding the amount of profit and loss is accepted with their oath, and he is not required to mention the matter in detail. His statement regarding loss and return to the partner is accepted. 'al-Nahr' in the chapter on partnership. 'If the loss exceeds etc.': For example, if the capital was one thousand and the profit one hundred, and the loss one hundred and fifty, the mudarib is not liable because he is a trustee; so he is not liable. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 247/2] Whether it was from his actions or not. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 386/7] 'And if they distributed the profit etc.': i.e., between the owner and the mudarib, and the mudarabah remained (they did not rescind it), then the entire capital or part of it was lost, they return the profit so that the owner takes his capital, because the profit is subsidiary as we mentioned, so the subsidiary cannot remain without the safety of the origin. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 247/2] And he said in al-Zayla'i on this issue: Because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'The believer is like a merchant: he is not safe until his capital is safe for him. Likewise, the believer is not safe for his obligations or (he said) his duties.' So if what is in his hand is lost, it becomes clear that it is not capital, and that what they distributed is not profit, because it is inconceivable that the subsidiary remains without the origin. So the mudarib guarantees what he took, and it is counted against him from the capital. Its analogy: the heirs setting aside part of the estate to pay the debt of the deceased, then distributing the rest of the estate. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 545/5] 'They return the profit etc.': So the mudarib guarantees what he took as profit because he took it for himself, unlike what remains in his hand, he does not guarantee it if he did not take it for himself. Similarly in al-Hamawi. (Fath) 'And what remains etc.': i.e., what remains of the capital after the return, it is between them, i.e., between the owner and the mudarib, because the owner has no right after taking his capital except in the profit. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 247/2] 'And if there is a deficiency etc.': i.e., if there is a deficiency in the capital such that the profit does not cover the loss, the mudarib is not liable for the deficiency, as we said that he is a trustee in it. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 248/2] 'And if they distributed the profit etc.': i.e., they distributed the profit between the mudarib and the owner, and then rescinded the mudarabah, then renewed it, then the capital after that was lost entirely or partially, they do not return the first profit because the first mudarabah ended with the rescission, and the second was established with a new contract. So the loss of capital in the second does not necessitate nullifying the first. So it is as if he paid him another capital. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 248/2]. 'They do not return etc.': because the first mudarabah ended with rescission and the second was established with a new contract, so loss in the second does not nullify the first. So it is as if he paid him another capital. And this is the legal device (hilah) if the mudarib fears that the profit will be taken back from him after distribution due to loss of what remains in his hand of capital. Its form: the mudarib returns the capital to the owner, then they distribute the profit, then the owner returns the capital to the mudarib and says: 'Work on mudarabah.' So that becomes an independent mudarabah. Similarly in al-Zayla'i. But his statement: 'the form of this legal device is that the mudarib returns the capital to the owner' suggests it is a condition, but it is not. Even if the capital after rescission remains in the mudarib's hand and the issue is the same, the ruling does not differ. (Fath)
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage covers several interconnected rulings about loss in mudarabah. Key principles: (1) Loss is always charged to profit first; profit is subsidiary (tabi') to capital (asl). (2) If loss exceeds profit, the mudarib is not liable for the shortfall because he is a trustee (amin). (3) If profit has been distributed but the mudarabah contract is still in effect and later capital is lost, the parties must return the distributed profit to restore the owner's capital. A hadith from al-Zayla'i is cited: 'The believer is like a merchant: not safe until his capital is safe.' (4) If the contract was rescinded and then a new mudarabah contract was made, and then capital is lost, the old profit is not returned because the contracts are separate. (5) A legal device (hilah) is described: if the mudarib fears having to return profit, he can return the capital to the owner, then distribute the profit, then the owner returns the capital to him as a new mudarabah. This makes the new contract independent, so subsequent losses do not affect the old profit. The commentary notes that this device is effective even if the capital remains in the mudarib's hand after rescission.

Key Terms
ربح (ribḥ): Profit generated from mudarabah capital, subsidiary to the principal.
تابع (tābiʿ): Subsidiary or dependent item; profit is subsidiary to capital.
أمين (amīn): Trustee; the mudarib is a trustee over the capital and is not liable for loss unless negligent.
حيلة (ḥīlah): Legal device to achieve a permissible outcome while avoiding a prohibition or undesired ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the mudarib returns the capital to the owner, they distribute the profit, and then the owner gives the capital back to the mudarib for a new mudarabah, what is the effect?
A) The old profit is secure and not returnable
B) The old profit must still be returned if any loss occurs
C) The original mudarabah continues
D) The owner forfeits his right to the profit

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi mudarabah, profit is subsidiary to capital, so loss is first covered by profit, and distributed profit must be returned if capital is later lost, unless the contract is rescinded and renewed.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
8 min
Return of Profit After Capital Loss in Mudarabah
Commentary on profit return when capital is lost after distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning profit in mudarabah when capital is lost after distribution, apply the principle of subsidiarity, and identify the legal stratagem (hila) to avoid the ruling.

Core Idea
Profit is subsidiary to capital; if capital is lost, profit must be returned to restore it.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains a key rule in mudarabah: if profit has been distributed but the capital is later destroyed, the profit must be returned. The commentary expands on the ruling, provides a hadith proof, and even offers a legal stratagem to avoid the consequence. Understanding this ruling illuminates the Hanafi principle of subsidiary (taba'iyyah) and the importance of preserving capital in investment partnerships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling scenario
Note the conditions: profit was distributed, the mudarabah remained intact (not dissolved), and later all or part of the capital was lost before the owner recovered his full capital.
Contrast with the later case where the contract is dissolved and renewed.
2
State the hukm and its level
The mudarib and owner must mutually return the profit (tarad) to restore the owner's capital. This is wajib because the hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and the principle of subsidiarity indicates the necessity.
The level is wajib, not fard, due to zanni evidence.
3
Analyze the proof
The dalil is a hadith narrated by al-Zayla'i: 'The example of a believer is like a merchant; he is not safe until his capital is safe...' This shows that profit (subsidiary) is only valid when capital (principal) is intact. The principle of taba'iyyah (subsidiarity) also supports this.
Both thubut and dalala are zanni, justifying the wajib level.
4
Apply the ruling to a deficiency scenario
If the profit is less than the loss, the mudarib is not liable for the shortfall because he is a trustee (amin). He only returns what he took as profit.
The mudarib's liability is limited to the profit he actually received.
5
Distinguish the dissolution and renewal case
If the contract is dissolved and a new mudarabah is initiated, then capital loss in the new contract does not require return of profit from the first contract. The first contract is completed and independent.
This distinction leads to the legal stratagem (hila) to protect the mudarib's profit.

أي رب المال والمضاربة

لم يترادا إلخ: لأن المضاربة الأولى قد انتهت بالفسخ وثبوت الثانية بعقد جديد، فهلاك المال في الثانية لا يوجب انتقاض الأولى، فصار كما إذا دفع إليه مالاً آخر، وهذه هي الحيلة فيما إذا خاف المضارب أن يسترد منه الربح بعد القسمة بسبب هلاك ما بقي في يده من رأس المال وصورة هذه أن يسلم المضارب رأس المال إلى رب المال ثم يقسمان الربح ثم يرد رب المال رأس المال إلى المضارب ويقول : اعمل على المضاربة فتكون تلك مضاربة مستقلة، كذا في "الزيلعي" لكن قوله : وصورة" هذه الحيلة أن يسلم المضارب رأس المال إلى رب المال" يوهم أنه شرط، وليس كذلك، حتى لو بقي المال بعد فسخ المضاربة في يد المضارب والمسألة بحالها لم يختلف الحكم. (فتح)
The book of Mudarabah: The mudarib partners with someone else. The mudarib is not liable. And if he distributed the profit and the mudarabah remained, then the capital or part of it was destroyed before the full recovery of the capital, they mutually return the profit so that the owner takes his capital, and what remains is between them. And if there is a deficiency, the mudarib is not liable for the deficiency from his capital. And if he distributed the profit and they dissolved [the contract], then they renewed it, and the capital was destroyed, they do not mutually return the first profit. Explanation: 'And if he distributed the profit etc.' means between the owner and the mudarib, and the mudarabah remained by not dissolving it, then all or part of the capital was destroyed, they (the owner and mudarib) mutually return the profit so that the owner takes his capital, because profit is subsidiary as we mentioned, so the subsidiary is not safe without the safety of the principal. From Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/247. And al-Zayla'i said in this matter: Because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'The example of a believer is like a merchant; he is not safe until his capital is safe for him, so likewise the believer is not safe for his...' or he said: 'his obligations'. So when what is in his hand is destroyed, it becomes clear that it is not the capital, and what they divided is not profit, since it is impossible for the subsidiary to remain without the principal. So the mudarib is liable for what the owner took, so it is counted against him from the capital. And its analogy is: the heirs setting aside some of the estate to pay a debt that [the deceased] took for himself, then dividing the rest of the estate. From Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 5/545. 'They mutually return the profit etc.' So the mudarib is liable for what he took as profit because he took it for himself, unlike what remained in his hand, he is not liable for it if he did not take it for himself. As in al-Hamawi. (Fath) 'And what remains etc.' means what remains of the capital after the mutual return is between them, i.e., between the owner and the mudarib, because the owner has no right after recovering his property except in profit. From Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/247. 'And if there is a deficiency etc.' means if there is a deficiency in the capital, i.e., the profit was not sufficient to cover the destroyed amount, the mudarib is not liable for the deficiency, because we stated that he is a trustee. From Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/248. 'And if he distributed the profit etc.' means if the profit was distributed between the mudarib and the owner, and they dissolved the mudarabah, then they renewed it (the owner and mudarib) a second time, then all or part of the capital was destroyed thereafter, they (the mudarib and owner) do not mutually return the first profit, because the first mudarabah ended with dissolution, and the second was established by a new contract, so the destruction of capital in the second does not necessitate the invalidation of the first. It becomes as if he gave him another property. 'They do not mutually return etc.' because the first mudarabah ended with dissolution and the second was established by a new contract, so the destruction of capital in the second does not necessitate the invalidation of the first. It becomes as if he gave him another property. This is the legal stratagem (hila) when the mudarib fears that the profit will be taken back from him after distribution due to the destruction of what remains in his hand of the capital. Its form is: the mudarib surrenders the capital to the owner, then they divide the profit, then the owner returns the capital to the mudarib and says: 'Work on mudarabah.' So that becomes an independent mudarabah. As in al-Zayla'i. But his statement 'the form of this hila is that the mudarib surrenders the capital to the owner' might imply it is a condition, but it is not. Even if the capital remained after dissolution in the hand of the mudarib and the matter is the same, the ruling does not differ. (Fath)
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding the ruling on profit distribution in mudarabah when capital is lost. The author first presents the base text: if profit was distributed while the mudarabah was still in effect and then the capital or part of it is destroyed before the owner recovers his full capital, the mudarib and owner must mutually return the profit to restore the capital. Any surplus after restoring the capital is divided between them as profit. If the profit was insufficient to cover the loss, the mudarib is not liable for the shortfall because he is a trustee. The commentary then explains the reasoning: profit is subsidiary to capital; therefore, the subsidiary cannot be valid if the principal is lost. A hadith is cited from al-Zayla'i: 'The example of a believer is like a merchant; he is not safe until his capital is safe for him.' This hadith supports the principle that profit depends on the safety of capital. The commentary also provides an analogy: if heirs set aside part of the estate to pay a debt the deceased took for himself, then divide the rest, it is similar. Next, the passage covers the case where the contract is dissolved and then renewed. If profit was distributed in the first contract, and then the contract was dissolved and a new mudarabah was initiated, and then capital is lost in the second contract, the profit from the first contract is not returned. This is because the first contract ended with dissolution, and the second is an independent contract. Finally, a legal stratagem (hila) is presented: if the mudarib fears that future losses might cause the profit he already took to be reclaimed, he can surrender the capital to the owner, divide the profit, and then have the owner return the capital with a new mudarabah contract. This makes the second mudarabah independent, so loss in the second does not affect the first. The condition that the capital be physically returned is not a strict requirement; the same ruling applies if the capital remains in the mudarib's hand after dissolution.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudārabah): A partnership contract where one party (owner) provides capital and the other (mudarib) provides labor, with profit shared as agreed.
رب المال (rabb al-māl): The owner of the capital in a mudarabah contract.
تراد (tarād): Mutual return of profit between the owner and mudarib to restore the capital when it is lost after distribution.
حيلة (ḥīlah): A legal stratagem to avoid the ruling that profit must be returned; here, formally ending the first contract and starting a new one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mudarib keeps the profit and the owner bears the loss.
B) The mudarib must return the profit to the owner to restore the capital.
C) The mudarib is not liable for anything because he is a trustee.
D) The mudarib and owner split the loss equally.

Key Takeaway
In an ongoing mudarabah, profit is subsidiary to capital; if capital is lost, profit must be restored.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

Returning Capital as Bida'ah Does Not Invalidate Mudarabah
Ruling with reasoning on validity of mudarabah when mudarib returns capital to owner as bida'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the mudarabah remains valid when the mudarib returns the capital to the owner as bida'ah and identify the condition that the mudarib must have received the capital first.

Core Idea
Returning capital as bida'ah does not invalidate mudarabah because the owner becomes an assistant.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: if the mudarib pays the capital back to the owner and the owner trades with it, the original mudarabah remains valid. The Hanafi reasoning is that the owner acts as an assistant (mu'in) to the mudarib, not as a partner. We will explore the principle and its application to different scenarios, including when the owner trades without the mudarib's order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What exactly does the mudarib do? He pays the capital to the owner as bida'ah (commission). The ruling: the mudarabah is not invalidated.
The text says 'wa la tafsid al-mudarabah' — the mudarabah remains valid (sahih).
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
Why is it not invalidated? Because the owner is an assistant (mu'in) to the mudarib in carrying out the work, and the capital is in his hand as bida'ah. The owner is not acting as a mudarib; he is helping.
The root word 'bida'ah' here means assistance, not a formal contract.
3
Consider the condition regarding receipt of capital
The mudarib must have received the capital first before giving it to the owner as bida'ah. If he makes it bida'ah before receiving, delivery is not a condition.
This ensures the mudarib has possession.
4
Distinguish scenarios when owner trades without mudarib's order
If the owner takes the capital without the mudarib's order and trades: if capital was cash, mudarabah is invalidated (owner acts for himself); if capital was goods (commodities), the mudarabah continues because the owner cannot annul the contract implicitly.
This shows the principle: owner as assistant only when he cannot be prevented.

Chapter: And the mudarabah is not invalidated by paying the capital to the owner as bida'ah (commission), i.e., by the mudarib's payment. The chapter: In this chapter, what was not mentioned at the beginning of mudarabah regarding the actions of the mudarib is mentioned, for added benefit and to draw attention to the intended repetition of the mudarib's actions. As in al-Nihayah. 'And the mudarabah is not invalidated etc.' because the owner is an assistant (mu'in) to the mudarib in carrying out the work, and the capital is in his hand as bida'ah. The word 'capital' is general, including all or part, as stated in al-Dhakhirah and al-Mabsut. The restriction to part found in al-Hidayah is coincidental, as stated in al-Nihayah. It is valid because... And he indicated by 'paying' that the mudarib must first receive the capital, such that if he made the capital bida'ah before receiving it, delivery is not a condition in it, as if the owner's work was stipulated from the beginning. And he qualified it by 'his payment' because if the owner takes the mudarabah capital without the mudarib's order and buys and sells, then the mudarabah is invalidated if the capital was cash, but if it became goods, it is not invalidated. In the first case, he works for himself, not as an assistant, so it is annulled. In the second, he does not have the power to annul explicitly, so likewise implicitly. So if he sells the goods for cash and then buys goods, the mudarib has his share of the profit from the first goods, not the second, because when he sold the goods and the capital became cash in his hand, that was an annulment of the mudarabah, so his purchase thereafter is for himself. If he sold the goods for similar goods, or for a measured or weighed item, it is between them as they stipulated, because the owner cannot annul the mudarabah as long as the capital is goods. And profit... The conclusion is that every disposition that becomes a right of the mudarib in a way that the owner cannot prevent him, the owner is an assistant therein, whether done by his order or without his order. And every disposition that the owner can prevent the mudarib from, the owner in that disposition is acting for himself unless it is by the mudarib's order, then he becomes an assistant. As in al-Mabsut. And his restriction to bida'ah is agreed upon, because if he paid the capital to the owner as mudarabah, the first mudarabah is not invalidated, but the second is invalidated, because mudarabah is a partnership on the owner's capital and the mudarib's labor, and there is no capital here. If we allowed it, it would lead to inversion of the subject. And when it is not valid, the owner's work remains by the mudarib's order, so the first mudarabah is not invalidated by it. As in al-Hidayah. By this it is known that it is bida'ah, even if called mudarabah, because what is meant by bida'ah here is assistance (isti'ana), since actual ibda' (giving something as bida'ah) is not possible here, i.e., the capital belongs to the owner (mubdi') and the work from the other, and there is no profit for the worker. And it is understood from the issue of the book the permissibility of ibda' with... and he is the father... to a stranger is more appropriate. The summary of what the mudarib has authority over is three types: a type he has authority over by absolute mudarabah, which is what is customary among merchants; a type he does not have authority over unless he says 'work with your opinion', such as mudarabah, partnership, and mixing; and a type he does not have authority over except by explicit permission, such as taking a loan, manumitting slaves absolutely, kitaba, lending, gifting, and charity. We have already presented their details at the beginning of the book. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7/386]
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This chapter (fasl) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with actions of the mudarib that were not covered at the start of the book. The main ruling is: the mudarabah is not invalidated if the mudarib pays the capital back to the owner as bida'ah (commission). The reason is that the owner becomes an assistant (mu'in) to the mudarib in carrying out the work, and the capital is in his hand as bida'ah, meaning he acts as a helper, not as a mudarib. The commentary explains that the capital given can be all or part of it, and this is agreed upon. It then discusses a scenario where the owner takes the capital without the mudarib's order and trades: if the capital was cash, the mudarabah is invalidated because the owner is acting for himself; if the capital was goods (commodities), the owner cannot invalidate the contract because he cannot unilaterally annul it, so the mudarabah continues. If the owner sells goods for cash and then buys goods, the profit from the first sale belongs to the mudarabah, but the profit from the subsequent purchase is for the owner alone, because the sale annulled the mudarabah (since cash was realized, the owner could then act for himself). The overarching principle is: any action that the mudarib has the right to do without the owner’s interference, the owner is an assistant; any action the owner can prevent, the owner is acting for himself unless the mudarib ordered it. The text then clarifies that calling this transaction 'bida'ah' is correct even if the parties call it 'mudarabah', because the intended meaning is assistance, not a true bida'ah where the worker gets no profit. Finally, the passage summarizes the mudarib’s authority into three categories: (1) Actions permitted absolutely under the mudarabah contract, i.e., customary trading practices. (2) Actions permitted only if the owner says 'work with your opinion' (e.g., entering into another mudarabah, partnership, mixing goods). (3) Actions that require explicit permission (e.g., borrowing, manumission, kitaba, lending, gifting, charity). These details are referenced from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Key Terms
بضاعة (biḍāʿah): A commission or consignment where the capital owner (mubdi') gives goods to another to trade, with no profit share for the worker.
إبضاع (ibḍāʿ): The act of giving something as bida'ah (commission).
مضاربة (mudārabah): A partnership contract between capital owner and worker, sharing profit as agreed.
الاستدانة (al-istidānah): Taking a loan on behalf of the partnership.
العتق (al-ʿitq): Manumission of a slave owned by the mudarabah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a mudarib returns capital to the owner as bida'ah, what is the status of the mudarabah?
A) It is invalidated (batil).
B) It remains valid (sahih).
C) It becomes a partnership.
D) It requires a new contract.

Key Takeaway
Returning capital to the owner as bida'ah does not invalidate the mudarabah; the owner becomes an assistant.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

Three Types of Mudarib's Authority
Classification of mudarib's authority into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the mudarib's actions into three authority levels and identify which actions require explicit permission.

Core Idea
The mudarib's authority is categorized into customary trade, actions needing owner's general permission, and actions requiring explicit permission.

In this lesson, we conclude the chapter by examining the summary of what the mudarib has authority over. The passage divides the mudarib's permissible actions into three types based on the level of permission required. Understanding this classification is essential for determining the validity of the mudarib's transactions and the resulting profit sharing.

Guided Path
1
List the three types
First, identify the three categories: (1) actions permitted by absolute mudarabah (customary trade), (2) actions permitted if the owner says 'work with your opinion' (e.g., entering another mudarabah, partnership, mixing), (3) actions requiring explicit permission (e.g., borrowing, manumission, kitaba, lending, gift, charity).
The text says 'wa hasil ma yamlukuhu al-mudarib thalaathat anwaa''.
2
Identify the basis for each category
Type 1: customary among merchants. Type 2: requires general authorization (اعمل برأيك). Type 3: requires explicit permission (بالصريح).
The difference lies in the level of owner's consent presumed.
3
Apply to examples
For each category, think of practical examples. Customary trade: buying and selling goods. 'Work with your opinion': the mudarib can set up a sub-mudarabah. Explicit permission: the mudarib cannot give charity from the capital without owner's consent.

Chapter: And the mudarabah is not invalidated by paying the capital to the owner as bida'ah (commission), i.e., by the mudarib's payment. The chapter: In this chapter, what was not mentioned at the beginning of mudarabah regarding the actions of the mudarib is mentioned, for added benefit and to draw attention to the intended repetition of the mudarib's actions. As in al-Nihayah. 'And the mudarabah is not invalidated etc.' because the owner is an assistant (mu'in) to the mudarib in carrying out the work, and the capital is in his hand as bida'ah. The word 'capital' is general, including all or part, as stated in al-Dhakhirah and al-Mabsut. The restriction to part found in al-Hidayah is coincidental, as stated in al-Nihayah. It is valid because... And he indicated by 'paying' that the mudarib must first receive the capital, such that if he made the capital bida'ah before receiving it, delivery is not a condition in it, as if the owner's work was stipulated from the beginning. And he qualified it by 'his payment' because if the owner takes the mudarabah capital without the mudarib's order and buys and sells, then the mudarabah is invalidated if the capital was cash, but if it became goods, it is not invalidated. In the first case, he works for himself, not as an assistant, so it is annulled. In the second, he does not have the power to annul explicitly, so likewise implicitly. So if he sells the goods for cash and then buys goods, the mudarib has his share of the profit from the first goods, not the second, because when he sold the goods and the capital became cash in his hand, that was an annulment of the mudarabah, so his purchase thereafter is for himself. If he sold the goods for similar goods, or for a measured or weighed item, it is between them as they stipulated, because the owner cannot annul the mudarabah as long as the capital is goods. And profit... The conclusion is that every disposition that becomes a right of the mudarib in a way that the owner cannot prevent him, the owner is an assistant therein, whether done by his order or without his order. And every disposition that the owner can prevent the mudarib from, the owner in that disposition is acting for himself unless it is by the mudarib's order, then he becomes an assistant. As in al-Mabsut. And his restriction to bida'ah is agreed upon, because if he paid the capital to the owner as mudarabah, the first mudarabah is not invalidated, but the second is invalidated, because mudarabah is a partnership on the owner's capital and the mudarib's labor, and there is no capital here. If we allowed it, it would lead to inversion of the subject. And when it is not valid, the owner's work remains by the mudarib's order, so the first mudarabah is not invalidated by it. As in al-Hidayah. By this it is known that it is bida'ah, even if called mudarabah, because what is meant by bida'ah here is assistance (isti'ana), since actual ibda' (giving something as bida'ah) is not possible here, i.e., the capital belongs to the owner (mubdi') and the work from the other, and there is no profit for the worker. And it is understood from the issue of the book the permissibility of ibda' with... and he is the father... to a stranger is more appropriate. The summary of what the mudarib has authority over is three types: a type he has authority over by absolute mudarabah, which is what is customary among merchants; a type he does not have authority over unless he says 'work with your opinion', such as mudarabah, partnership, and mixing; and a type he does not have authority over except by explicit permission, such as taking a loan, manumitting slaves absolutely, kitaba, lending, gifting, and charity. We have already presented their details at the beginning of the book. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7/386]

Commentary
This chapter (fasl) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with actions of the mudarib that were not covered at the start of the book. The main ruling is: the mudarabah is not invalidated if the mudarib pays the capital back to the owner as bida'ah (commission). The reason is that the owner becomes an assistant (mu'in) to the mudarib in carrying out the work, and the capital is in his hand as bida'ah, meaning he acts as a helper, not as a mudarib. The commentary explains that the capital given can be all or part of it, and this is agreed upon. It then discusses a scenario where the owner takes the capital without the mudarib's order and trades: if the capital was cash, the mudarabah is invalidated because the owner is acting for himself; if the capital was goods (commodities), the owner cannot invalidate the contract because he cannot unilaterally annul it, so the mudarabah continues. If the owner sells goods for cash and then buys goods, the profit from the first sale belongs to the mudarabah, but the profit from the subsequent purchase is for the owner alone, because the sale annulled the mudarabah (since cash was realized, the owner could then act for himself). The overarching principle is: any action that the mudarib has the right to do without the owner’s interference, the owner is an assistant; any action the owner can prevent, the owner is acting for himself unless the mudarib ordered it. The text then clarifies that calling this transaction 'bida'ah' is correct even if the parties call it 'mudarabah', because the intended meaning is assistance, not a true bida'ah where the worker gets no profit. Finally, the passage summarizes the mudarib’s authority into three categories: (1) Actions permitted absolutely under the mudarabah contract, i.e., customary trading practices. (2) Actions permitted only if the owner says 'work with your opinion' (e.g., entering into another mudarabah, partnership, mixing goods). (3) Actions that require explicit permission (e.g., borrowing, manumission, kitaba, lending, gifting, charity). These details are referenced from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Key Terms
بضاعة (biḍāʿah): A commission or consignment where the capital owner (mubdi') gives goods to another to trade, with no profit share for the worker.
إبضاع (ibḍāʿ): The act of giving something as bida'ah (commission).
مضاربة (mudārabah): A partnership contract between capital owner and worker, sharing profit as agreed.
الاستدانة (al-istidānah): Taking a loan on behalf of the partnership.
العتق (al-ʿitq): Manumission of a slave owned by the mudarabah.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
6 min
Mudarabah not invalidated by returning capital as bida'a
Ruling that mudarabah remains valid when mudarib gives capital to owner as bida'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why returning mudarabah capital to the owner as bida'a does not invalidate the contract, and apply the critical distinction between owner as assistant vs. independent trader.

Core Idea
Returning capital as bida'a turns the owner into an assistant – mudarabah remains valid.

In a mudarabah, the mudarib manages the capital. But what happens if the mudarib simply hands the capital back to the owner? Surprisingly, the contract remains intact – if the owner holds it as bida'a (goods). This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning behind this ruling and clarifies when the owner's actions break the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The mudarib gives the capital to the owner as bida'a. The ruling: the mudarabah is not invalidated; it remains valid (sahih).
2
Understand the legal rationale
The owner becomes an assistant (mu'in) to the mudarib in performing the work. The capital is held as goods, not forming a new contract.
The word 'money' (mal) is unrestricted, covering all or part of the capital.
3
Distinguish owner's independent action
If the owner takes the capital without permission and trades with cash, the mudarabah is nullified. If he trades with goods, it remains valid because goods cannot implicitly dissolve the contract.
The passage details the profit consequences of such actions.
4
Apply the scope of the rule
The rule applies to both full and partial return of capital. Any restriction to 'part' (as in al-Hidayah) is accidental.
Al-Nihayah clarifies the apparent restriction is not a valid ikhtilaf.
5
Note the mudarib's authority types
The passage concludes with three levels of mudarib authority: customary actions allowed by default, actions requiring permission to use own judgment, and actions requiring explicit authorization.
This contextualizes the mudarib's power to return capital.
