Book of Mudarabah. Actions of the Mudarib. Section: And the mudarabah is not invalidated by giving the capital to the owner as a bida'a (i.e., by the mudarib giving [it]). Section: He mentioned in this section what he did not mention at the beginning of the book regarding the actions of the mudarib, as an addition for benefit, and to alert that the actions of the mudarib are intended to be repeated, as in al-Nihayah. And the mudarabah is not invalidated, etc., because the owner of the capital is an assistant to the mudarib in performing the work, and the capital is in his hand on the basis of bida'a. The word 'capital' is unrestricted, so it includes all and part, and this is explicitly stated in al-Dhakhirah and al-Mabsut. What occurred in al-Hidayah regarding restriction to part is accidental, as stated in al-Nihayah. It is valid because... He indicated by 'giving' that the mudarib must first receive the capital, such that if he makes the capital a bida'a before receiving it, delivery is not a condition in it, just as if the owner's work is stipulated from the beginning. And it is restricted by his giving; because if the owner takes the mudarabah capital without the mudarib's permission and sells and buys, then the mudarabah is nullified if the capital is cash, but not if it becomes goods; because in the first he is acting for himself, not as an assistant, so it is dissolved, and in the second he does not have the power to dissolve explicitly, nor implicitly. So if he sells the goods for cash, then buys goods, the mudarib has his share of the profit from the first goods, not the second; because when he sold the goods and the capital became cash in his hand, that was a dissolution of the mudarabah, so his purchase thereafter is for himself. So if he sells goods for similar goods, or for something measured or weighed, it is between them according to what they stipulated; because the owner is not able to dissolve the mudarabah as long as the capital is goods. And the conclusion is that every transaction that becomes a right of the mudarib in a way that the owner cannot prevent, then the owner in that is an assistant to him, whether he does it by his command or without his command. And every transaction that the owner can prevent the mudarib from, then the owner in that transaction is acting for himself, unless it is by the mudarib's command, then he is an assistant to him, as in al-Mabsut. And its restriction to bida'a is accidental; because if he gives the capital to the owner as a mudarabah, the first mudarabah is not invalidated, but the second is invalidated; because mudarabah is a partnership on the owner's capital and the mudarib's work, and there is no capital here. So if we allowed it, it would lead to inverting the subject. And when it is not valid, the owner's work remains by the mudarib's command, so the first mudarabah is not invalidated by it, as in al-Hidayah. And by this it is known that it is a bida'a, even if it is called mudarabah; because what is meant by bida'a here is assistance; because literal ibda' is not possible here, that the capital belongs to the one giving it and the work is from another, with no profit for the worker. And from the issue of the book, the permissibility of ibda' with the father is understood, and with a non-relative a fortiori. And the summary of what the mudarib is entitled to is three types: a type he is entitled to by absolute mudarabah, which is what is customary among traders; a type he is not entitled to unless told 'act according to your opinion', like mudarabah, partnership, and mixing; and a type he is not entitled to unless explicitly stated, like borrowing, manumission absolutely, kitabah (contract of manumission), lending, gift, and charity. We have presented their details at the beginning of the book. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/386]
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq). The section addresses actions of the mudarib (silent partner) in a mudarabah (silent partnership). The core ruling is that giving the capital back to the owner (rabb al-mal) as a bida'a (goods/merchandise) does not invalidate the mudarabah. The reasoning is that the owner becomes an assistant (mu'in) to the mudarib in performing work, and the capital remains in the owner's hand on the basis of bida'a, not a new partnership. The author explains that the ruling is unconditional, covering both all or part of the capital, contrary to a possible misreading in al-Hidayah. He then distinguishes cases: if the owner takes the capital without permission and trades with cash, the mudarabah is null; if with goods, it remains valid because goods cannot dissolve the contract implicitly. The profit in such cases is detailed: only from the first set of goods. The passage concludes by categorizing the mudarib's powers: (1) customary actions allowed by default, (2) actions requiring permission to use own judgment (like entering new mudarabah or partnership), and (3) actions requiring explicit authorization (like borrowing or manumission).

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership contract where one party provides capital (rabb al-mal) and the other (mudarib) provides work, with profit shared as agreed.
بضاعة (bida'a): Goods or merchandise; here it means the owner holds the capital as goods (not as a new partnership), acting as an assistant.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The owner of the capital in a mudarabah contract.
مضارب (mudarib): The worker/entrepreneur in a mudarabah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the mudarabah if the mudarib gives the capital back to the owner as a bida'a?
A) It is invalidated immediately
B) It remains valid, and the owner becomes an assistant
C) It becomes a new mudarabah
D) The mudarib is no longer entitled to profit

Key Takeaway
Giving mudarabah capital to the owner as bida'a does not dissolve the contract; the owner acts as assistant, unless he independently trades with cash.

Mudarib's Travel Expenses: Hanafi vs Shafi'i vs Maliki
Ruling on mudarib's expenses during travel, with Shafi'i and Maliki positions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mudarib's travel expenses, explain the istihsan basis, and compare it with the Shafi'i and Maliki positions, identifying the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
The mudarib's travel expenses are taken from the mudarabah capital because he is detained for the owner's benefit — istihsan overrides qiyas.

This passage addresses a key practical issue in mudarabah contracts: who bears the mudarib's travel expenses? The Hanafi school, using istihsan, treats the mudarib like a detained worker, obligating the capital to cover his essentials. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools differ. Understanding this dispute reveals how different usul (istihsan vs qiyas) yield different fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: the mudarib's food, drink, clothing, and ride are taken from the mudarabah capital absolutely (whether capital is little or much) by istihsan.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Shafi'i holds the mudarib spends from his own wealth absolutely. Maliki holds it depends: if capital is much, from the capital; if little, from his own.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Shafi'i argues the mudarib is acting for himself, so expenses are his own. Maliki analogizes to a partner (no expenses from partnership capital unless customarily agreed).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafi responds that the mudarib is detained (mahbus) from his own livelihood for the owner's benefit, analogous to a judge or wife. This detention (ihtibas) obligates the capital to provide maintenance. Custom also supports it.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, the mudarib can take expenses from the capital; under Shafi'i, he cannot; under Maliki, it depends on the amount of capital. This affects the mudarib's net profit and liability.

Book of Mudarabah. 1.2 Actions of the Mudarib. If he travels, then his food, drink, clothing, and ride are from the mudarabah capital, and if he works in the city or in a village... If he travels, etc.: He said in al-Fath: This is when he travels only with the mudarabah capital. If he travels with his own wealth and the mudarabah capital, or mixes them with the owner's permission, or travels with two capitals for two men, he spends proportionally, as in al-Sharnablaliyyah from Sharh al-Majma'. If he travels, etc.: We restricted it to the mudarib, because the hired worker (ajir), agent (wakil), and one given goods as bida'a (mustabdi') have no expenses at all; because the hired worker is entitled to wages unconditionally, and the agent and mustabdi' are volunteers (mutabarri'an). Similarly, the partner, if he travels with partnership capital, has no expenses because it is not customary, mentioned in al-Kafi. Al-Nihayah explicitly states it is obligatory in partnership capital. The word 'mudarabah' was used absolutely, so it refers to a valid mudarabah, because the mudarib in a void one is a hired worker, with no expenses. Since the cause for the obligation of expenses is [being detained], it is understood that what is meant by 'travel' is not the legal travel measured by three days, but rather that he cannot spend the night in his home. Even if he leaves the city but can return within the night, he is like in the city, no expenses. The word 'city' is absolute, so it includes his birthplace and the city he has taken as a domicile. But if he intends to stay in a city without making it a domicile, he gets expenses, as in Sharh al-Majma'. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/387] He detains himself from the destitute. His food and drink, etc.: i.e., the mudarib's food, drink, clothing, and ride on the road are from the mudarabah capital, because expenses become obligatory due to detention, like the expenses of a judge or a wife. When he travels, he becomes detained by it, so his regular maintenance becomes due. Ramp;z al-Haqa'iq: 2/248. He said in Mulla: Miskin: and washing of clothes he wears, the wage of a servant who serves him, fodder for the animal he rides, oil for a place he needs like cupping (hijamah), and bathhouse fee are also from the mudarabah capital, end. He said in al-Fath: because cleanliness of body and clothes causes many to deal with him, for a dirty person is considered by people... and they shun dealing with him. So all of that is permitted to him with moderation; if he exceeds, he is liable. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 5/551] Since the custom of traders is considered, he may eat fruit even if not from expenses, and he may dye (with henna). And he indicated by his saying 'his food' that he eats what is customary [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/388]. The restriction to customary is explicit in prohibiting non-customary food. And his saying: 'since the custom of traders is considered' etc. indicates that eating fruit is also restricted to customary. 'And his ride' (rukub, with fathah on ra) is what he rides, whether purchased or hired. In the mudarabah capital, etc., absolutely, whether the capital is little or much, by istihsan according to us. And al-Shafi'i said: the mudarib spends from his own wealth absolutely, whether [the capital] is little or much. And Malik said: he spends from the mudarabah capital if the capital is much, and if little, from his own wealth, as in Mulla Miskin. 'And if he works in the city', etc., i.e., if the mudarib works in the city or in a village, his expenses are from his own wealth, not from the mudarabah capital. (Miskin) And he said in al-Fath in explaining the city: whether he was born in it or made it a domicile. But if he intends to stay in a city without making it a domicile, he gets expenses, al-Bahr from Sharh al-Majma'. So if he takes capital in Kufa while he is from Basra, and had come to Kufa as a traveler, he has no expenses from the capital as long as he is in Kufa. When he leaves Kufa traveling, he gets expenses.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the mudarib's expenses during travel. It is from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The Hanafi position is that when a mudarib travels solely for the mudarabah, his essential expenses (food, drink, clothing, transportation) are taken from the mudarabah capital, regardless of the amount, by istihsan. The rationale is that the mudarib is effectively detained (mahbus) from his own livelihood, analogous to a judge or a wife. The Hanafis argue that cleanliness and customary needs (like servant, fodder, bath) are also covered, all within reason. If he exceeds, he is liable. The Shafi'i school holds that the mudarib always spends from his own wealth, while Malik distinguishes based on the amount of capital. The passage also clarifies that 'travel' here means being unable to return home for the night, not the three-day legal travel. If the mudarib works in his city (or a city he has made his domicile), he gets no expenses because he is not detained. The text also notes that similar expenses for an agent, hired worker, or a regular partner are not covered.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The entrepreneur/silent partner in a mudarabah contract.
نفقة (nafaqah): Necessary expenses for the mudarib during travel, including food, drink, clothing, and transportation.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; the basis for allowing expenses from the capital against strict qiyas.
احتباس (ihtibas): Detention; the mudarib is considered detained from his own work due to the mudarabah travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the mudarib's food and drink when he travels for mudarabah?
A) He must pay from his own wealth
B) He may take from the mudarabah capital only if the capital is large
C) He may take from the mudarabah capital without restriction (by istihsan)
D) He is not entitled to any expenses

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows the mudarib's travel expenses from the capital by istihsan, based on detention (ihtibas), unlike Shafi'i (always from own) and Maliki (conditional on capital size).

Mudarib's Travel Expenses: Ruling, Conditions, and School Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute and expansion of the Hanafi ruling on mudarib's travel expenses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the mudarib's travel expenses, identify the condition of travel (inability to return overnight), explain the basis of istihsan via detention analogy, and distinguish the Shafi'i and Maliki positions.

Core Idea
The mudarib's travel expenses are wajib from the mudarabah capital due to detention — but only if he cannot return home overnight.

In mudarabah, when the mudarib (worker) travels for the partnership, who should bear his food, drink, clothing, and riding expenses? The Hanafi school holds that these are wajib from the mudarabah capital itself, based on the principle of istihsan and the analogy of detention. However, the Shafi'i and Maliki schools disagree. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, its conditions, and the inter-madhhab dispute, drawing from the text of Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the ruling (hukm) on the mudarib's expenses during travel? The Hanafi school says: food, drink, clothing, and riding are wajib from the mudarabah capital.
Note the hukm level is wajib, not fard — it's an obligation arising from the contract and custom, not from a definitive textual command.
2
Determine the condition of travel
The text clarifies that 'travel' does not mean a three-day journey. Instead, it means the mudarib cannot return to his home and spend the night there. If he can return the same night, he is like a resident and gets no expenses.
This is a key Hanafi definition: expenses are tied to detention, not to distance.
3
Understand the legal analogy (qiyas khafi / istihsan)
Why are expenses from the capital? Because the mudarib is detained (mahbus) for the sake of the capital during travel. This is analogous to a judge's maintenance (paid from public funds) or a wife's maintenance (paid by husband).
The basis is istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas (which would treat the mudarib as a trustee bearing own expenses).
4
Compare with the opposing schools
Al-Shafi'i holds the mudarib must pay from his own money absolutely. Malik distinguishes: if the capital is abundant, expenses from capital; if little, from mudarib's own money.
The Hanafi rebuttal: the mudarib is not a volunteer; he is forced to travel for the capital, so expenses fall on the capital.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In Hanafi, the mudarib can directly take reasonable expenses from the capital. In Shafi'i, he cannot; he must use his own funds and cannot claim reimbursement. In Maliki, it depends on the capital's size.
This affects the mudarib's liquidity and risk in the partnership.

فإن سافر فطعامه وشرابه وكسوته وركوبه في مال المضاربة. قال في "الفتح": هذا إذا سافر بمال المضاربة فقط، ولو سافر بماله ومال المضاربة، أو خلطه بإذن رب المال، أو سافر بمالين لرجلين أنفق بالحصة. وقيدنا بالمضارب؛ لأن الأجير والوكيل والمستبضع لا نفقة لهم مطلقاً؛ لأن الأجير يستحق البدل لا محالة، والوكيل والمستبضع متبرعان، وكذا الشريك إذا سافر بمال الشركة لا نفقة له؛ لأنه لم يجر التعارف به. وأطلق المضاربة، فانصرفت إلى الصحيحة؛ لأن المضارب في الفاسدة أجير لا نفقة له. ولما كانت العلة في وجوب النفقة لأجلها، علم أن ليس المراد بالسفر السفر الشرعي المقدر بثلاثة أيام، بل المراد أن لا يمكنه أن يبيت في منزله، وإن خرج من المصر، وأمكنه أن يعود إليه في ليلة، فهو كالمصر لا نفقة له. وأطلق المصر، فشمل مصره الذي ولد فيه، والمصر الذي اتخذه دارا، أما لو نوى الإقامة بمصر، ولم يتخذه دارا، فله النفقة. فطعامه وشرابه إلخ: أي فطعام المضارب وشرابه وكسوته وركوبه في الطريق في مال المضاربة؛ لأن النفقة تجب بسبب الاحتباس كنفقة القاضي والزوجة، فإذا سافر صار محبوسا به، فتجب مؤنته الراتبة. قال في "ملا" مسكين: وغسل ثياب يلبسها وأجرة أجير يخدمه، وعلف دابة يركبها، والدهن في موضع يحتاج إليه كالحجام، وأجرة الحمام في مال المضاربة أيضاً. قال في "الفتح": لأن نظافة البدن والثياب توجب كثرة من يعامله؛ لأن صاحب الوسخ يعده الناس من فيجتنبون معاملته، فيطلق له من ذلك كله بالمعروف، حتى إذا زاد يضمن. ولما كان المعتبر عادة التجار كان له أكل الفاكهة وإن لم تكن من النفقة وله الخضاب. وأشار بقوله: "فطعامه" إلى أنه يأكل ما كان معتاده. والتقييد بالمعتاد صريح في المنع من غير المعتاد من الطعام. وقوله: "ولما كان المعتبر عادة التجار" إلخ يشير إلى أن التفكه مقيد بالمعتاد أيضاً. وركوبه بفتح الراء أي ما يركب، سواء كان بشراء أو كراء. في مال المضاربة إلخ مطلقاً مال المضاربة قليلاً أو كثيراً استحساناً عندنا، وقال الشافعي: ينفق المضارب من مال نفسه مطلقاً، وقال مالك: إن كثر المال ينفق من مال المضاربة، ولو قل فمن مال نفسه. كذا في "ملا مسكين".
If he travels, his food, drink, clothing, and riding are from the mudarabah capital. He said in al-Fath: This is if he travels with the mudarabah capital alone; if he travels with his own capital and the mudarabah capital, or mixes them with the permission of the capital owner, or travels with two capitals for two men, he spends proportionally. We restrict to the mudarib because the hired worker, agent, and consignee have no expenses at all; because the hired worker receives compensation in any case, and the agent and consignee are volunteers. Likewise, a partner if he travels with partnership capital has no expenses because custom has not established it. He said: He absolutely mentioned mudarabah, so it applies to the valid mudarabah; because the mudarib in an invalid mudarabah is a hired worker with no expenses. Since the reason for the obligation of expenses is detention, we know that what is meant by travel is not the legal travel estimated at three days, but rather that he cannot spend the night at home; if he goes out of the city but can return to it in the same night, he is like [someone in] the city and has no expenses. The city is absolute, so it includes the city where he was born and the city he has adopted as home; if he intends to reside in a city but has not adopted it as home, he has expenses. His food, etc.: that is, the mudarib's food, drink, clothing, and riding on the road are from the mudarabah capital because expenses are obligatory due to detention, like the expenses of a judge and a wife; when he travels, he becomes detained by it, so his regular maintenance is obligatory. He said in Mulla Miskin: Washing of clothes he wears, hire of a servant who serves him, fodder for the animal he rides, oil in a place where he needs it like a barber, and bathhouse fee are also from the mudarabah capital. He said in al-Fath: Because cleanliness of body and clothes increases the number of people who deal with him; because people consider the unclean person as [unacceptable] and avoid dealing with him, so all that is given to him in a reasonable manner, and if he exceeds, he guarantees. Since the custom of traders is considered, he may eat fruit even if it is not from provisions, and he may dye his hair. And he indicated by his statement 'his food' that he eats what is customary for him. The restriction to customary is explicit in prohibiting non-customary food. And his statement 'since the custom of traders is considered' etc. indicates that indulgence in fruit is also restricted to what is customary. And 'riding' with a fatha on the ra' means the mount, whether by purchase or hire. In the mudarabah capital etc., absolutely the mudarabah capital, whether little or much, as istihsan according to us. And al-Shafi'i said: The mudarib spends from his own capital absolutely. And Malik said: If the capital is much, he spends from the mudarabah capital; if little, from his own capital. Thus in Mulla Miskin.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the mudarib's expenses during travel. The Hanafi position, stated in the matn, is that the mudarib's food, drink, clothing, and riding are taken from the mudarabah capital. The commentary defines the conditions: the travel must be exclusively for the mudarabah capital; if he mixes capitals, he spends proportionally. The reason for the obligation is detention (ihtibas) of the mudarib for the sake of the capital, analogous to a judge's or wife's maintenance. The key point is that the travel need not be a full three-day legal journey; rather, it is defined by the inability to return home and spend the night. If he can return within the same night, he is treated as being in the city and gets no expenses. The Hanafi school applies istihsan to allow expenses from the capital regardless of the amount, while al-Shafi'i requires the mudarib to pay from his own money, and Malik distinguishes based on the abundance of capital. The commentary also details what constitutes necessary expenses, including washing, servant hire, fodder, oil, and bathhouse fees, all limited to customary (mut'ad) amounts.

Key Terms
المضارب (al-mudarib): The worker in a mudarabah partnership, who manages the capital and shares in the profit.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance expenses including food, drink, clothing, and riding during travel for the mudarabah.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; the basis for the Hanafi ruling allowing expenses from capital regardless of amount.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's travel expenses are wajib from the mudarabah capital due to detention, but only if he cannot return home overnight – a key Hanafi detail that differs from other schools.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
beginner

Mudarib's Expenses When Working in the City
Ruling on mudarib's city expenses with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mudarib's expenses when working in the city and apply the definition of 'misr' to determine when expenses are from the capital.

Core Idea
A mudarib pays his own expenses in his hometown; only travel for business shifts expenses to capital.

In a mudarabah partnership, a common question is who bears the mudarib's living expenses. The Hanafi school provides a clear rule: when working in his own city, the mudarib's expenses are from his own pocket. But what qualifies as 'his own city'? This lesson examines the definition and the exceptions through a detailed example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage begins with the general rule: if the mudarib works in the city or village, his expenses are from his own money, not the capital.
This is the foundational ruling from the matn.
2
Define 'misr' (city)
The commentary explains that 'misr' means the city where the mudarib was born or has adopted as a permanent home (watan asli). If he intends to reside but has not made it his home, he is still considered a traveler and gets expenses.
The distinction between watan asli and watan iqamah is crucial.
3
Apply the exception
If the mudarib is in a city as a traveler (even if he temporarily resides), he is entitled to expenses from the capital for his stay and travel.
This exception is because his presence is for the business, not for his own home.
4
Work through the example
The example: a Basran takes capital in Kufa. While in Kufa as a traveler, no expenses from capital. When he leaves for Basra, he gets expenses. When in Basra (hometown), no expenses. When he leaves Basra for business, he gets expenses again.
The example illustrates the application of the rule to a specific scenario.
5
Conclude the principle
Travel for the mudarabah business triggers capital expenses; staying in one's hometown does not.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن عمل في المصر أو في قرية فنفقته في ماله. أي وإن عمل المضارب في المصر أو في قرية، فنفقته في ماله لا في مال المضاربة. قال في "الفتح" في شرح المصر: سواء ولد فيه أو اتخذه داراً، أما إذا نوى الإقامة بمصر ولم يتخذه داراً، فله النفقة. فلو أخذ مالاً بالكوفة وهو من أهل البصرة، وكان قدم الكوفة مسافراً، فلا نفقة له في المال ما دام بالكوفة، فإذا خرج منها مسافراً، فله النفقة حتى يأتي البصرة؛ لأن خروجه لأجل المال، ولا ينفق من المال ما دام بالبصرة؛ لأن البصرة وطن أصلي له، فكان إقامته فيه لأجل الوطن لا لأجل المال، فإذا خرج من البصرة له أن ينفق من المال إلى أن يأتي الكوفة؛ لأن خروجه من البصرة لأجل المال وله أن ينفق أيضاً ما أقام بالكوفة حتى يعود إلى البصرة؛ لأن وطنه بالكوفة كان وطن إقامة وإنه يبطله بالسفر. كالدواء: أي نفقة المضارب إذا عمل في المصر أو في قرية في مال نفسه كالدواء؛ فإنه في ماله مطلقا، وعن أبي حنيفة: أن الدواء في مال المضاربة؛ لأنه لإصلاح بدنه، وكذلك النورة والدهن في قولهما، خلافا لمحمد في الدهن. ووجه ظاهر الرواية كما ذكره الزيلعي: أن النفقة معلوم وقوعها، والحاجة إلى الدواء من العوارض، فكان موهوماً، فلا يجب كما في حق المرأة.
And if he works in the city or village, his expenses are from his own money. That is, if the mudarib works in the city or village, his expenses are from his own money, not from the mudarabah capital. He said in al-Fath in explaining the city: whether he was born there or adopted it as home; if he intends to reside in a city but has not adopted it as home, he has expenses. So if he takes capital in Kufa while he is from Basra, and he had come to Kufa as a traveler, he has no expenses from the capital as long as he is in Kufa; but when he leaves it as a traveler, he has expenses until he reaches Basra, because his departure is for the sake of the capital. He does not spend from the capital as long as he is in Basra because Basra is his original homeland, so his stay there is for the homeland, not for the capital. If he leaves Basra, he may spend from the capital until he comes to Kufa, because his departure from Basra is for the capital. And he may also spend while staying in Kufa until he returns to Basra, because his homeland in Kufa was a residence homeland, and he cancels it by traveling. Like medicine: that is, the mudarib's expenses when working in the city or village are from his own money, like medicine; it is absolutely from his own money. And from Abu Hanifa: that medicine is from the mudarabah capital because it is for the correction of his body; likewise, depilatory and oil according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad regarding oil. And the preferred view of the zahir al-riwayah, as al-Zayla'i mentioned: that expenses are known to occur, while the need for medicine is incidental, so it is speculative, thus not obligatory as in the case of a wife.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage contains two main rulings. First, when the mudarib works in the city (misr) or village (qarya), his expenses are from his own money. The commentary defines the term 'misr' as the city where he was born or which he has adopted as home. If he intends to reside in a city but has not made it his home, he is considered a traveler and gets expenses from the capital. The example of a Basran taking capital in Kufa illustrates the application: while in Kufa as a traveler, he gets no expenses (as he is in a city he has not adopted as home), but when he leaves for Basra, he gets expenses. Second, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding medicine: according to the prevailing view (the zahir al-riwayah), medicine is from the mudarib's own money, just like other expenses in the city. However, Abu Hanifa holds that medicine is from the mudarabah capital because it is necessary for the body's well-being. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree with him on medicine and depilatory (nurah) but disagree on oil (duhn). The preferred view is that medicine is not from the capital because the need for it is incidental, unlike regular expenses.

Key Terms
المصر (al-misr): The city where the mudarib was born or has adopted as a permanent home (watan asli).
الوطن الأصلي (al-watan al-asli): Original homeland; the place where a person was born or has permanently settled.
وطن الإقامة (watan al-iqamah): Residence homeland; a place where a person intends to stay temporarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, from whose money does the mudarib pay his expenses when working in the city?
A) From the mudarabah capital, because he is working.
B) From his own money.
C) From a joint account.
D) From the profits only.

Key Takeaway
A mudarib's personal expenses in his hometown are his own responsibility, not the capital's.

The Medicine Dispute: From the Mudarabah Capital or Mudarib's Own Money?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on medicine expenses in mudarabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on medicine expenses in mudarabah, explain the reasoning behind each, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Medicine, like city expenses, is from the mudarib's own pocket — but Abu Hanifa dissents, and the preferred view prevails.

The previous lesson established that regular city expenses are from the mudarib's own money. The same rule applies to medicine, according to the majority of the Hanafi school. However, a notable intra-madhhab difference exists. This lesson examines the arguments and the preferred fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed item
The passage specifically discusses medicine (dawa'), and also mentions depilatory (nurah) and oil (duhn).
The dispute extends to these related items.
2
State the zahir al-riwayah position
The apparently transmitted ruling (zahir al-riwayah) is that medicine is absolutely from the mudarib's own money, just like other city expenses.
This is the position of the majority of Hanafi scholars.
3
State Abu Hanifa's opposing view
Abu Hanifa holds that medicine is from the mudarabah capital because it is for the correction of his body, analogous to travel expenses which are for the capital.
Abu Hanifa views medicine as a necessary expense for the mudarib to continue working.
4
Note the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree with Abu Hanifa on medicine and depilatory (nurah), but Muhammad disagrees regarding oil (duhn).
This shows a nuanced internal difference.
5
Understand the tarjih reasoning
The preferred view (zahir al-riwayah) is supported by the reasoning that regular expenses are certain to occur, while the need for medicine is incidental and speculative. An analogy is drawn to the case of a woman (wife) where medicine is not obligatory.
Thus, medicine is not a burden on the capital.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he works in the city or village, his expenses are from his own money. That is, if the mudarib works in the city or village, his expenses are from his own money, not from the mudarabah capital. He said in al-Fath in explaining the city: whether he was born there or adopted it as home; if he intends to reside in a city but has not adopted it as home, he has expenses. Commentary
This passage contains two main rulings. First, when the mudarib works in the city (misr) or village (qarya), his expenses are from his own money. The commentary defines the term 'misr' as the city where he was born or which he has adopted as home. الدواء (al-dawa'): Medicine; its expense is debated whether from capital or mudarib's own pocket.
النورة (al-nurah): Depilatory; included in the dispute by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
الدهن (al-duhn): Oil; Muhammad excludes it from the capital.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
8 min
Recouping Mudarib's Expenses from Profit
Ruling statement explaining the mechanism for recouping mudarib travel expenses from profit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that the mudarib's travel expenses are recouped from profit before capital is shared and apply it to a mudarabah scenario.

Core Idea
In mudarabah, expenses are treated as loss – profit first restores the capital, then is shared.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains a key mechanism in mudarabah: how the mudarib's authorized travel expenses are handled when profit appears. The Hanafi school prioritizes capital preservation: profit is first used to replenish any capital spent by the mudarib, and only then is the remaining profit divided. Let's trace the logic and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its rationale
The passage states that if profit occurs, the owner recoups the mudarib's expenses from the profit until the capital is complete. The rationale: expenses are analogous to a loss, and losses are charged to profit, not capital.
This reflects the principle that profit is subsidiary to capital.
2
Apply the loss analogy
The mudarib's spent amount is considered 'as if lost.' Since losses are borne by profit, the recoupment naturally comes from profit first. If profit is sufficient, capital is restored; if not, the unrecovered loss reduces the capital.
3
Determine the order of distribution
The passage implies a hierarchy: (1) recoup expenses from profit to restore capital, (2) then divide any remaining profit according to the stipulated ratio. If no profit remains, nothing is shared.
4
Recognize the condition on the mudarib
The phrase 'if he profits' indicates that the mudarib may spend before profit appears, but if no profit ever materializes, the mudarib owes nothing. The loss is borne by the capital owner.
5
Distinguish from debt
The passage notes that debts (e.g., purchases on credit with permission) take precedence even over capital restoration. This separates the treatment of expenses (treated as loss) from obligations (treated as liabilities).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ربح أخذ المالك ما أنفق من رأس المال. أي فإن ربح المضارب بعد ما أنفق من رأس المال على نفسه أخذ المالك قدر ما أنفق المضارب من رأس المال حتى يتم به رأس المال، فإذا استوفى رأس المال، وفضل شيء، يكون بينهما على ما شرطا فتكون النفقة مصروفة إلى الربح؛ لأن ما أنفقه يجعل كالهالك، والهالك يصرف إلى الربح، ولا تكون مصروفة إلى رأس المال؛ لأن رأس المال أصل والربح تبع، فلا يسلم لهما التبع حتى يسلم لرب المال الأصل، وفي قوله: "فإن ربح" إشارة إلى أن للمضارب أن ينفق على نفسه المضاربة في السفر قبل الربح، وإلى أنه لو لم يظهر ربح لا شيء على المضارب. ما أنفق : قيد بالنفقة؛ لأنه لو كان في المال دين غيرها، قدم إيفاؤه على رأس المال، كما إذا استدان المضارب بإذن صريح من رب المال، أو اشترى شيئاً بأكثر من رأس المال.
If he profits, the owner takes what was spent from the capital. That is, if the mudarib profits after having spent from the capital on himself, the owner takes the amount that the mudarib spent from the capital until the capital is completed thereby; when the capital is recovered, and there is something left, it is between them as they stipulated. So the expense is charged to the profit because what he spent is treated as if it were lost, and a loss is charged to the profit. It is not charged to the capital because the capital is the principal and profit is subsidiary; the subsidiary is not secure until the principal is secure for the capital owner. In his statement: 'if he profits' there is an indication that the mudarib may spend on himself from the mudarabah [capital] during travel before profit, and that if no profit appears, nothing is owed by the mudarib. What was spent: restricted to expenses; because if there is a debt in the capital other than that, its repayment takes precedence over the capital, as when the mudarib borrows with explicit permission of the capital owner, or buys something for more than the capital.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement explaining the mechanism for recouping the mudarib's travel expenses. The key principle is that the mudarib may spend from the capital during travel even before any profit appears. However, when profit does occur, the owner first deducts the amount spent by the mudarib from the capital before dividing the profit according to their agreement. This is because the spent amount is treated as a loss, and losses are borne by the profit, not the capital. So if the capital is not intact, profit is used to replenish it first. If no profit ever appears, the mudarib is not liable for the expenses he took (since they were allowed). The passage also distinguishes expenses from debts: if the mudarib takes a loan from the capital with permission, that debt is repaid before capital recovery.

Key Terms
ربح (ribh): Profit generated by the mudarabah; it is first used to replenish the capital from the mudarib's expenses.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital amount of the mudarabah; it must be recovered before profit is divided.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When profit appears in a mudarabah, how are the mudarib's travel expenses handled according to the Hanafi school? A) They are deducted from the mudarib's share of profit. B) They are considered part of the capital and must be repaid to the owner first. C) They are charged to the profit, and only after the capital is fully recovered is the remaining profit shared. D) They are ignored and the mudarib pays them from his own pocket.
A) They are deducted from the mudarib's share of profit.
B) They are considered part of the capital and must be repaid to the owner first.
C) They are charged to the profit, and only after the capital is fully recovered is the remaining profit shared.
D) They are ignored and the mudarib pays them from his own pocket.

Key Takeaway
In mudarabah, the mudarib's travel expenses are recouped from profit before any division, preserving the capital intact.
